Reasons for Rejecting the Simple Meaning Definition
Author explains three reasons for rejecting the simple meaning definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three reasons scholars rejected the simple meaning definition of nazar and why they restricted the definition to one type.

Core Idea
Simple meanings vary; only compound meanings allow choice in both transitions.

In defining nazar (theological reflection), scholars considered a simple meaning definition but found it problematic. The author of Sharh al-Mawaqif explains three key objections: lack of regulation, limited role of technical discipline, and limited role of choice. This passage clarifies why the definition was narrowed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal English translation. Notice the structure: each objection is introduced by 'قوله' (his statement).
The passage is a commentary on the original text, explaining each phrase.
2
Identify the three reasons
Find each of the three distinct reasons presented: 1) lack of regulation, 2) limited role of discipline, 3) limited role of choice. Understand each one.
The third reason contrasts simple and compound meanings.
3
Understand the concluding restriction
Read the final sentence: scholars restricted the definition to one of the two types of nazar. Think about why this prevents the definition from being too broad.
This ensures the definition is precise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لم ينضبط إلخ) لأن المعاني البسيطة التي تستلزم الانتقال إلى أخرى، تختلف بحسب اختلاف الأشخاص والعرف والعادات وليس له ضابط يعرف به ذلك . قوله : ( للصناعة إلخ) إذ مدخليتها فيه إنما هو باعتبار استفادة المناسبة للمطلوب، دون الصورة وأكثر مسائل الصناعة تتعلق بالصورة. قوله : ( وللاختيار إلخ) إذ الاختيار فيه إنما هو في الانتقال من المطلوب المشعور به إلى المبدأ، والانتقال منه إلى المطلوب يترتب من غير اختيار بخلاف المعاني المركبة، فإن للاختيار فيهما مدخلاً بعد حصول المبادي من جهة الترتيب بينهما . قوله : ( وخصوا إلخ) فهو تعريف لأحد قسمي النظر لا لمطلقه، حتى لا يكون جامعاً .
His statement: (not regulated etc.) because the simple meanings that necessitate transition to others differ according to differences of persons, custom, and habits, and there is no standard by which that is known. His statement: (for discipline etc.) since its involvement in it is only on account of deriving suitability for the desired, not the form, and most issues of the discipline pertain to form. His statement: (and for choice etc.) since choice in it is only in transitioning from the perceived desired to the principle, and transitioning from it to the desired follows without choice, unlike compound meanings, for choice has involvement in both after obtaining the principles in terms of ordering between them. His statement: (and they restricted etc.) so it is a definition for one of the two types of nazar, not for its absolute, so that it is not comprehensive.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage explains three reasons why the simple meaning definition was not adopted. First, simple meanings are not regulated; they vary by person, custom, and habit, so there is no universal standard. Second, technical discipline (صناعة) only contributes to assessing suitability (مناسبة) for the desired conclusion, not the form of reasoning; most of the discipline deals with form. Third, choice only plays a role in the first transition (from desired to principle), not in the subsequent transition from principle to desired, which is automatic. In contrast, compound meanings allow choice in both transitions after principles are obtained. Finally, the scholars restricted the definition of nazar to one of its two types (the one that is regulated) so that the definition would not be all-encompassing.

Key Terms

صناعة
اختيار
مناسبة
صورة
معاني مركبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The simple meaning definition was rejected only because it lacked regulation.
Correction: The author gives two additional reasons: limited role of technical discipline and limited role of choice.

Checkpoint
Why was the simple meaning definition not adopted? Choose the best answer.
A) It was too simple.
B) It was not regulated due to variation in persons and customs.
C) It did not lead to the desired conclusion.
D) It lacked a clear principle.

Key Takeaway
The simple meaning definition was rejected because simple meanings are unregulated and because both technical discipline and choice have only limited roles; scholars therefore restricted the definition to one type of nazar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The lesson shows that choice only operates in the first transition for simple meanings, leaving the conclusion automatic. In your spiritual life, where have you allowed automatic conclusions to take over without exercising choice? Identify one matter of faith where you need to insert active choice between your principles and your conclusions.
?
The scholars restricted the definition to avoid overgeneralization. Is there an area of your understanding of Islam where you have adopted an overly broad definition or principle without considering its limitations? First Condition: Qualifying Zann with Mutabiqa
The author argues that the definition must be for absolute nazar, requiring a condition to exclude material corruption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of nazar must qualify 'zann' with 'mutabiqa' (conformity) to exclude materially corrupt nazar.

Core Idea
Absolute nazar includes both sound and corrupt; a condition filters out false premises.

The author explains that the definition covers all types of nazar, not just sound ones. To exclude nazar that is corrupt due to false premises (material corruption), the opinion (zann) must be qualified as conforming to reality and appropriate to the desired conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its literal translation. Notice the key terms like māddah and mutābiqah.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two conditions mentioned
The author states that without qualifying zann, corrupt nazar would not be excluded. Find the two conditions: (1) qualifying zann with mutabiqa, (2) the condition of leading (ta'di).
We focus on the first here.
3
Understand why the first condition is needed
The definition is for absolute nazar. If it were only for sound nazar, we would need to add 'mutabiqa' to exclude cases where the premises are false. Consider the author's explanation of mutabiqa as conformity to reality and suitability to the desired.
This condition ensures that even if premises are true but irrelevant, they are still corrupt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيهما أنه (أي الحد المذكور) تعريف لمطلق النظر الشامل لجميع أقسامه (لا للصحيح منه) فقط وإلا وجب تقييد الظن المذكور في الحد بالمطابقة ليخرج عنه النظر الفاسد بحسب مادته (و) وجب أيضاً أن يوضع في الحد. قوله: (وإلا وجب تقييد الظن بالمطابقة) لعله أراد بالمطابقة المطابقة لنفس الأمر، بأن تكون تلك الأمور المظنونة صادقة فيها، وللمطلوب بأن تكون مناسبة له، وإلا فالصادق في نفس الأمر الغير المناسب للمطلوب لا يخرج باعتبار قيد المطابقة أنه فاسد بحسب المادة كما مع سيأتي. قوله : ( ووجب أيضاً أن يوضع إلخ) فيه بحث لأن المفهوم منه وجوب اعتبار الأمرين معاً، مع أن الأمر الثاني مغن عن الأول، إذ الفاسد بحسب المادة لا يؤدي كما سيصرح به في المقصد الذي يليه، ويمكن أن يقال : النظر الفاسد بحسب المادة قد يؤدي نفسه نحو زيد حمار وكل حمار جسم، والكلام هاهنا مبني عليه وأما ما سيذكره من أن النظر الصحيح هو الذي يؤدي إلى المطلوب والفاسد ما يقابله، فالمراد : هناك هو الذي يؤدي نوعه فلا مخالفة ولا محذور بقى فيه بحث ،آخر وهو أن وجوب القيد الثاني إنما يرد إذا كان المراد من قوله للتأدي ليتأدى أو ليحصل التأدي، أما لو كان المراد التعليل بمعنى لكون تلك الأمور المؤدية إلى المطلوب، فلا يرد إلا أن يقال : المعنى الأول هو المتبادر من عبارة التعريف فليفهم.
And the second [point] is that it (i.e., the mentioned definition) is a definition of absolute nazar encompassing all its divisions, not only sound nazar; otherwise it would be necessary to qualify the 'zann' mentioned in the definition with 'mutabiqa' (conformity) to exclude nazar that is corrupt according to its matter. And it is also necessary to place in the definition... His statement: (otherwise it would be necessary to qualify zann with mutabiqa) perhaps he intended by mutabiqa conformity to reality, in that those matters of opinion are true in it, and for the desired to be suitable for it; otherwise, that which is true in reality but not suitable for the desired does not exit [from the definition] by the consideration of the condition of conformity, being corrupt according to matter, as will come. His statement: (and it is also necessary to place etc.) there is discussion about it because the understood from it is the necessity of considering both matters together, while the second matter suffices without the first, since the corrupt according to matter does not lead [to the desired] as he will clarify in the following section. And it can be said: corrupt nazar according to matter may itself lead, for example 'Zayd is a donkey and every donkey is a body', and the discussion here is based on it. As for what he will mention that sound nazar is that which leads to the desired and corrupt is its opposite, the intended there is that which leads in its type, so there is no contradiction and no problem. There remains another discussion: that the necessity of the second condition only applies if the intended from his statement 'li-l-taaddi' is 'to lead' or 'so that leading occurs'. But if the intended is the causal meaning 'because those matters are leading to the desired', then it does not apply, except that it is said: the first meaning is the apparent from the phrase of the definition, so understand.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage has two main points. First, the definition given earlier is for absolute nazar, not just sound nazar. To exclude corrupt nazar (materially corrupt, i.e., false premises), the term 'zann' must be qualified with 'mutabiqa' (conformity to reality). Second, there is also a need for a second condition 'li-l-taaddi' (leading). The author discusses whether both conditions are necessary; he suggests that the second condition (leading) might suffice because materially corrupt reasoning does not lead to the desired conclusion. However, he notes that sometimes materially corrupt reasoning can accidentally lead (like 'Zayd is a donkey, every donkey is a body' leads to 'Zayd is a body' but is unsound). The author clarifies that when they say 'sound nazar leads', they mean 'leads in its type', not necessarily every instance. He also discusses the meaning of 'li-l-taaddi': whether it means 'to lead' or 'because of being leading', preferring the first.

Key Terms
مطلق النظر
صحيح
فاسد

مطابقة

Why is it necessary to qualify 'zann' with 'mutabiqa' in the definition of nazar?
A) To include all types of nazar.
B) To exclude nazar that is corrupt in matter.

Key Takeaway

Second Condition: Is the 'Leading' Condition Redundant?
The author discusses whether the second condition is necessary and clarifies its meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the discussion about whether a second condition (li-l-ta'di) is necessary or if the first condition suffices, and the author's resolution.

Core Idea
The second condition may seem redundant but clarifies that sound nazar leads in its type.

The author examines whether requiring nazar to 'lead' (ta'di) to the desired is necessary, given that materially corrupt nazar may coincidentally lead. He resolves that sound nazar is defined by its type, not every instance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the discussion about whether the second condition is needed.
Pay attention to the example 'Zayd is a donkey...'.
2
Understand the two possible meanings of 'li-l-ta'di'
The author considers two interpretations: (1) 'to lead' as a purpose, or (2) 'because of being leading' as a cause. This affects whether the condition is redundant.
The first meaning is preferred.
3
Grasp the resolution about 'type'
The author explains that when they say 'sound nazar leads', they mean 'leads in its type' (i.e., the type of reasoning generally leads), not that every instance must lead. This resolves the apparent contradiction.
Materially corrupt nazar may accidentally lead, but its type does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيهما أنه (أي الحد المذكور) تعريف لمطلق النظر الشامل لجميع أقسامه (لا للصحيح منه) فقط وإلا وجب تقييد الظن المذكور في الحد بالمطابقة ليخرج عنه النظر الفاسد بحسب مادته (و) وجب أيضاً أن يوضع في الحد. قوله: (وإلا وجب تقييد الظن بالمطابقة) لعله أراد بالمطابقة المطابقة لنفس الأمر، بأن تكون تلك الأمور المظنونة صادقة فيها، وللمطلوب بأن تكون مناسبة له، وإلا فالصادق في نفس الأمر الغير المناسب للمطلوب لا يخرج باعتبار قيد المطابقة أنه فاسد بحسب المادة كما مع سيأتي. قوله : ( ووجب أيضاً أن يوضع إلخ) فيه بحث لأن المفهوم منه وجوب اعتبار الأمرين معاً، مع أن الأمر الثاني مغن عن الأول، إذ الفاسد بحسب المادة لا يؤدي كما سيصرح به في المقصد الذي يليه، ويمكن أن يقال : النظر الفاسد بحسب المادة قد يؤدي نفسه نحو زيد حمار وكل حمار جسم، والكلام هاهنا مبني عليه وأما ما سيذكره من أن النظر الصحيح هو الذي يؤدي إلى المطلوب والفاسد ما يقابله، فالمراد : هناك هو الذي يؤدي نوعه فلا مخالفة ولا محذور بقى فيه بحث ،آخر وهو أن وجوب القيد الثاني إنما يرد إذا كان المراد من قوله للتأدي ليتأدى أو ليحصل التأدي، أما لو كان المراد التعليل بمعنى لكون تلك الأمور المؤدية إلى المطلوب، فلا يرد إلا أن يقال : المعنى الأول هو المتبادر من عبارة التعريف فليفهم.
And the second [point] is that it (i.e., the mentioned definition) is a definition of absolute nazar encompassing all its divisions, not only sound nazar; otherwise it would be necessary to qualify the 'zann' mentioned in the definition with 'mutabiqa' (conformity) to exclude nazar that is corrupt according to its matter. And it is also necessary to place in the definition... His statement: (otherwise it would be necessary to qualify zann with mutabiqa) perhaps he intended by mutabiqa conformity to reality, in that those matters of opinion are true in it, and for the desired to be suitable for it; otherwise, that which is true in reality but not suitable for the desired does not exit [from the definition] by the consideration of the condition of conformity, being corrupt according to matter, as will come. His statement: (and it is also necessary to place etc.) there is discussion about it because the understood from it is the necessity of considering both matters together, while the second matter suffices without the first, since the corrupt according to matter does not lead [to the desired] as he will clarify in the following section. And it can be said: corrupt nazar according to matter may itself lead, for example 'Zayd is a donkey and every donkey is a body', and the discussion here is based on it. As for what he will mention that sound nazar is that which leads to the desired and corrupt is its opposite, the intended there is that which leads in its type, so there is no contradiction and no problem. There remains another discussion: that the necessity of the second condition only applies if the intended from his statement 'li-l-taaddi' is 'to lead' or 'so that leading occurs'. But if the intended is the causal meaning 'because those matters are leading to the desired', then it does not apply, except that it is said: the first meaning is the apparent from the phrase of the definition, so understand.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage has two main points. First, the definition given earlier is for absolute nazar, not just sound nazar. To exclude corrupt nazar (materially corrupt, i.e., false premises), the term 'zann' must be qualified with 'mutabiqa' (conformity to reality). Second, there is also a need for a second condition 'li-l-taaddi' (leading). The author discusses whether both conditions are necessary; he suggests that the second condition (leading) might suffice because materially corrupt reasoning does not lead to the desired conclusion. However, he notes that sometimes materially corrupt reasoning can accidentally lead (like 'Zayd is a donkey, every donkey is a body' leads to 'Zayd is a body' but is unsound). The author clarifies that when they say 'sound nazar leads', they mean 'leads in its type', not necessarily every instance. He also discusses the meaning of 'li-l-taaddi': whether it means 'to lead' or 'because of being leading', preferring the first.

صحيح
فاسد
نوع

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the second condition (li-l-ta'di) not make the first condition redundant?
A) Because materially corrupt nazar never leads to the desired.
B) Because sound nazar is defined as leading in its type.
C) Because the first condition is about mutabiqa, not about leading.
D) Because the author rejects the second condition.

Key Takeaway
The condition that nazar leads to the desired is understood as applying to the type of reasoning, not every individual instance, so it remains necessary alongside the condition of conformity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a belief because the conclusion sounded true, even though the reasoning you used was flawed? How does this lesson's insistence on soundness 'in its type' challenge you to inspect the very structure of your arguments, not just their outcomes?
?
This lesson shows that even corrupt reasoning can accidentally lead to truth. In your own search for certainty about life's biggest questions—like the existence of God or the truth of Islam—are you willing to examine whether your reasoning is sound in its type, or have you been content with arguments that merely 'work' for you? What will you change starting today?

Objection of Comprehensiveness and Broadening 'Ilm and Zann
The author addresses the objection that the definition may not cover unknown premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definition of nazar is defended against the objection that it fails to cover unknown premises by expanding the meanings of 'ilm and zann.

Core Idea
A definition must encompass all cases; expanding terms can save it.

This lesson examines an objection to the definition of nazar as arranging known or conjectured premises. Critics argue that some premises are neither known nor conjectured—they are unknown with compound ignorance. The author responds by reinterpreting 'ilm and zann to include all types of cognition, showing the definition is comprehensive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by a response.
Pay attention to key terms like 'ilm, zann, and compound ignorance.
2
Identify the objection
The objection states that the definition may not be comprehensive because some premises are neither known nor conjectured but unknown with compound ignorance.
Compound ignorance means being unaware of one's ignorance.
3
Identify the author's response
The author broadens the meanings of 'ilm and zann: 'ilm includes conception and certain affirmation, while zann includes all non-certain affirmations (including compound ignorance).
Thus, all premises are covered under the definition.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider why definitions in kalam must be comprehensive. How does this response preserve the definition for all types of reasoning?
This shows the flexibility of terminology in Islamic logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بحيث يؤدي ليخرج عنه النظر الفاسد بحسب صورته) وإذا كان هذا التعريف لمطلق النظر فمقدماته قد لا تكون معلومة ولا مظنونة أيضاً بل هي مجهولة جهلاً مركباً، فلا يكون التعريف جامعاً، ولا يمكن أن يحمل العلم على المعنى الأعم، إذ يلزم أن يكون قوله أو مظنونة مستدركاً، نعم قد يقال : كما أن الظن يطلق على المعنى المشهور كذلك يطلق على ما يقابل اليقين من التصديقات، فيحمل العلم هاهنا على ما يتناول التصور، والتصديق اليقيني والظن على ما يتناول سائر التصديقات. ونقول : نحن في تعريف النظر على مذهبهم بحيث يتناول جميع أقسامه في التصورات والتصديقات بلا إشكال.
His statement: (such that it leads to exclude nazar corrupt according to its form) and if this definition is for absolute nazar, then its premises may not be known nor conjectured (maznuna) but rather unknown with compound ignorance, so the definition would not be comprehensive, and it is not possible to interpret 'ilm in the more general meaning, for then his statement 'or maznuna' would be redundant. Yes, it may be said: just as zann is applied to the well-known meaning, likewise it is applied to what is opposite of yaqin (certainty) among affirmations (tasdiqat), so 'ilm is interpreted here as encompassing conception (tasawwur) and certain affirmation, and zann as encompassing all other affirmations. And we say: we, in defining nazar according to their school, [hold] that it encompasses all its divisions in conceptions and affirmations without problem.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage deals with an objection to the definition of nazar as 'arranging known or conjectured matters such that they lead'. If the definition is for absolute nazar, then some premises may be neither known nor conjectured but simply unknown with compound ignorance, making the definition not comprehensive. The response is to expand the meanings of 'ilm and zann. 'Ilm here includes conception (tasawwur) and certain affirmation, while zann includes all non-certain affirmations (including compound ignorance). Thus, the definition covers all types of premises.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of nazar only covers premises that are known or conjectured in the strict sense.
Correction: The definition covers all premises because 'ilm and zann are broadened to include conception, certain affirmation, and all non-certain affirmations, including compound ignorance.

How does the author resolve the objection that the definition may not include unknown premises?

C) By limiting the definition to sound nazar.

Key Takeaway
The definition of nazar is defended by broadening the scope of 'ilm and zann to cover all types of premises.

The author broadens the terms 'ilm and zann to include ignorance. Is there a core concept in your faith or worldview that you hold rigidly, even when faced with evidence that requires expansion? Are you willing to let go of a narrow definition to preserve the truth?

Expanded Meanings of 'Ilm and Zann in the Definition of Nazar
The author clarifies the expanded meanings of 'ilm and zann in the definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the terms 'ilm and zann are defined broadly in the definition of nazar, and how this allows the definition to cover all its instances without exception.

Core Idea
The definition of nazar works only when 'ilm and zann are understood broadly.

In this passage, the author clarifies the key terms used in the definition of nazar. He explains that 'ilm and zann are taken in their most general senses so that the definition encompasses all forms of intellectual reasoning. Without these expanded meanings, the definition would leave out many valid cases.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the literal English translation. Let the author's own phrasing come through before you analyze.
Notice the concise style: each term is glossed briefly.
2
Identify the three glosses
Find where the author explains 'al-ma'na al-a'am', 'al-ma'na al-mashhur', and 'ma yuqabil al-yaqin'. Note how each is defined.
These glosses set the stage for the broader definitions.
3
List the three types of non-certain belief
Under 'ma yuqabil al-yaqin', the author lists three types: zann (preponderant belief), compound ignorance, and the belief of the muqallid. Understand each.
This shows that 'zann' in the definition is not just preponderant belief but includes all non-certain affirmations.
4
Contrast with 'ilm
Notice how 'ilm is defined broadly to include conceptions and certain affirmations, i.e., everything that is not the three types of non-certain belief. This contrast ensures the definition covers all cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( على المعنى الأعم) أي الصورة الحاصلة . قوله : ( على المعنى المشهور أعني الاعتقاد الراجح . مر، قوله : ( على ما يقابل اليقين أي الاعتقاد الذي لا يكون جازماً مطابقاً ثابتاً سواء كان غير جازم، أو جازماً غير مطابق أو جازماً مطابقاً غير ثابت فيتناول الظن بالمعنى المشهور، والجهل المركب واعتقاد المقلد وبقرينة المقابلة يحمل العلم على ما عداها، وهو التصورات والتّصديقات اليقينية، فحينئذ يشمل التعريف جميع أفراده من غير استدراك قيد من القيود .
His statement: (on the more general meaning) i.e., the acquired form. His statement: (on the well-known meaning, namely the preponderant belief). It has passed. His statement: (on what is opposite of yaqin) i.e., belief that is not decisive, conforming, and stable, whether it is non-decisive, or decisive but non-conforming, or decisive and conforming but not stable. So it includes zann in the well-known sense, compound ignorance, and the belief of the muqallid (blind follower). And by the context of comparison, 'ilm is taken to encompass what is other than these, namely conceptions and certain affirmations. Then the definition includes all its individuals without any extra condition.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage explains the technical terms used in the response. The author glosses 'al-ma'na al-a'am' as the acquired form (acquired knowledge). Then he clarifies 'al-ma'na al-mashhur' of zann as preponderant belief. Then he defines 'ma yuqabil al-yaqin' as belief that lacks any one of the three conditions of certainty: decisiveness, conformity, and stability. This includes zann (preponderant but not certain), compound ignorance (decisive but false), and the belief of a blind follower (decisive and true but unstable). Thus, the expanded meaning of zann covers all non-certain affirmations. By contrast, 'ilm is taken to include conceptions and certain affirmations. With these broad definitions, the definition of nazar as arranging known or conjectured matters covers all cases.

جازم
مطابق
ثابت

تصورات
تصديقات يقينية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think zann only refers to preponderant belief.
Correction: Here zann is defined broadly to include all non-certain affirmations: preponderant belief, compound ignorance, and the belief of the muqallid.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does 'zann' in the definition include?
A) Only preponderant belief.
B) Only non-decisive belief.
C) All affirmations except certain ones.
D) Only compound ignorance.

Key Takeaway
To understand the definition of nazar, one must know that 'ilm and zann are used in their broadest senses, encompassing all certain and non-certain elements of thought.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
You rely on zann (preponderant opinion) in many daily matters—but are you aware that this same category includes the muqallid's faithlessness? Is your own faith stable because it is based on evidence, or merely because it feels familiar?
?

The Scope of Nazar: Broadening Knowledge and Opinion
Sanusi resolves a scope issue in the definition of nazar by expanding the meanings of 'ilm' and 'zann'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definition of nazar accommodates premises that are not certainly known by interpreting 'knowledge' and 'probable opinion' in broader senses.

Core Idea
Nazar's definition can include all types of reasoning by expanding what counts as knowledge and probable opinion.

Sanusi addresses an objection that the definition of nazar fails when premises are unknown. He shows that by taking 'knowledge' to include conception and certain assent, and 'probable' to cover all other assents, the definition becomes comprehensive. This lesson explores that interpretive move.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the objection and the proposed solution. Note the key terms 'ilm' and 'zann'.
The passage begins by stating an objection about premises being unknown.
2
Identify the three types of belief opposing certainty
The author lists three types: not decisive, decisive but not conforming, and decisive and conforming but not fixed. These correspond to probable opinion, compound ignorance, and blind following.
This enumeration is central to the broader interpretation.
3
Understand the comprehensiveness
Realise how this reinterpretation allows the definition to include all divisions of nazar in conceptions and assents without any redundant condition.
The context of contrast between 'knowledge' and 'probable' is key.

قوله : ( ووجب أيضاً أن يوضع إلخ) فيه بحث لأن المفهوم منه وجوب اعتبار الأمرين معاً، مع أن الأمر الثاني مغن عن الأول، إذ الفاسد بحسب المادة لا يؤدي كما سيصرح به في المقصد الذي يليه، ويمكن أن يقال : النظر الفاسد بحسب المادة قد يؤدي نفسه نحو زيد حمار وكل حمار جسم، والكلام هاهنا مبني عليه وأما ما سيذكره من أن النظر الصحيح هو الذي يؤدي إلى المطلوب والفاسد ما يقابله، فالمراد : هناك هو الذي يؤدي نوعه فلا مخالفة ولا محذور بقى فيه بحث ،آخر وهو أن وجوب القيد الثاني إنما يرد إذا كان المراد من قوله للتأدي ليتأدى أو ليحصل التأدي، أما لو كان المراد التعليل بمعنى لكون تلك الأمور المؤدية إلى المطلوب، فلا يرد إلا أن يقال : المعنى الأول هو المتبادر من عبارة التعريف فليفهم . قوله : (بل هي مجهولة أو مقلداً فيها .
قوله : ( كذلك يطلق على ما يقابل اليقين من التصديقات أشار بقوله من التصديقات إلى خروج الشك والوهم، إذ لا يطلق النظر على ترتيب الأمور المشكوكة والموهومة، ثم هذا المعنى يحتمل أن يكون هو المتعارف عند من عرف النظر، بما ذكر وإن كان المعنى الآخر هو هو المشهور العامة، ولو سلم فالقرينة قائمة على إرادته فلا ضير في استعماله في التعريف
The fifth observation - first aim: on its definition. Place of his statement 'lilta'di': his statement: 'so that it leads' to exclude invalid reasoning according to its form. And if this definition is for absolute nazar, its premises may be neither known nor even probable, but rather unknown with compound ignorance, so the definition would not be comprehensive, and it is not possible to interpret 'knowledge' (al-'ilm) in the more general sense, because then his statement 'or probable' would be redundant. Yes, it may be said: just as 'probable opinion' (al-zann) is applied to the well-known meaning, similarly it is applied to what opposes certainty (al-yaqin) among assents. So 'knowledge' here is taken to include conception and certain assent, and 'probable opinion' to include all other assents. We say: we are in the definition of nazar according to their school such that it includes all its divisions in conceptions and assents without problem. His statement: 'in the more general sense' i.e., the form obtained. His statement: 'in the well-known meaning' i.e., the preponderant belief. His statement: 'to what opposes certainty' i.e., belief that is not decisive, conforming, and fixed, whether it is not decisive, or decisive but not conforming, or decisive and conforming but not fixed. So it includes probable opinion in the well-known sense, compound ignorance, and the belief of the blind follower. By the context of contrast, 'knowledge' is taken to mean what is other than these, i.e., conceptions and certain assents. Then the definition includes all its individuals without any redundant condition. Those probable matters are true in themselves, and for the sought matter they must be appropriate to it; otherwise, the true in itself but inappropriate for the sought matter is not excluded by the condition of conformity because it is invalid materially as will come. His statement: 'and it also must be placed etc.' have discussion because what is understood from it is the necessity of considering both matters together, while the second dispenses with the first, since the materially invalid does not lead, as he will clarify in the following aim. It can be said: materially invalid reasoning may itself lead, like 'Zayd is a donkey, and every donkey is a body', but the discussion here is built on it. As for what he will mention, that correct reasoning leads to the sought and invalid is its opposite, what is meant there is what its type leads to, so there is no contradiction or problem. There remains another discussion: the necessity of the second condition only occurs if what is meant by his statement 'lilta'di' is 'to lead' or 'to obtain leading'; if what is meant is the cause in the sense of those matters leading to the sought, then it is not necessary, except it be said: the first meaning is the apparent one from the wording of the definition, so understand. His statement: 'rather they are unknown or blind imitation in them.' His statement: 'likewise it is applied to what opposes certainty among assents' he indicated by his statement 'among assents' the exclusion of doubt and illusion, for nazar is not applied to the arrangement of doubtful and illusory matters. Then this meaning is likely to be the conventional one among those who defined nazar as mentioned, even if the other meaning is the common one among the general public, and if granted, the context indicates its intent, so there is no harm in using it in the definition.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the conditions and scope of the definition of nazar (theoretical reflection). The author begins by addressing an objection: if the definition requires premises that are known or at least probable, then if premises are unknown (with compound ignorance), the definition fails to include that case. He then notes that 'knowledge' (al-'ilm) cannot be taken in its broadest sense (including conception) because that would make the mention of 'probable' redundant. The solution is to interpret 'knowledge' as including conception and certain assent, and 'probable' as including all other assents (like probable opinion, compound ignorance, and blind acceptance). Thus the definition covers all types of nazar. The author then discusses the necessity of the condition that the premises lead to the sought (al-ta'di), and debates whether the second condition (material validity) is needed. He clarifies that the discussion is about the type of reasoning, not each individual instance. Finally, he addresses the point that 'probable' is used to mean what opposes certainty, and that it is acceptable because the context clarifies the intent. The commentary proceeds by explaining each quoted statement in sequence, resolving potential contradictions.

Conditions of Nazar: Leading and Material Validity
Sanusi debates the necessity of both leading to the sought and material validity in nazar's definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether the condition of 'leading to the sought' is sufficient or if material validity is also necessary in the definition of nazar.

Core Idea
Not all valid reasoning leads to the sought—material appropriateness is key.

Sanusi continues by examining the conditions of nazar, particularly the relationship between the condition that it leads to the sought (al-ta'di) and material validity. He discusses a potential redundancy and clarifies that the discussion concerns the type of reasoning, not each instance. This lesson delves into that sophisticated debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
The passage refers to two conditions: that nazar leads to the sought (al-ta'di) and that it is materially valid (al-mutabaqa). Notice the discussion about their necessity.
The second condition is about the premises being appropriate to the sought.
2
Understand the objection
An objection is raised that the second condition is redundant because a materially invalid reasoning cannot lead to the sought. But the author gives a counterexample.
Example: 'Zayd is a donkey, every donkey is a body' leads to 'Zayd is a body' but is materially invalid.
3
Resolve the redundancy issue
The resolution is that the discussion is about the type of reasoning: correct reasoning by type leads to the sought, but materially invalid reasoning can still lead in individual instances. The condition 'leading to the sought' is interpreted in terms of type.
This avoids contradiction.
4
Examine the interpretation of 'lilta'di'
The author discusses whether 'lilta'di' means 'to lead' or 'to be a cause of leading'. The necessity of the second condition depends on this interpretation.
The first meaning is considered more apparent.

The Formal Definition of Nazar
Sanusi defines nazar and explains its components and exclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of nazar as deliberate mental observation aimed at acquiring new knowledge, and how it excludes intuition and presential knowledge.

Core Idea
Nazar is the deliberate use of prior knowledge to reach something unknown.

Sanusi provides a careful definition of nazar, the foundational method of kalam. He specifies that it is an intentional act of the rational soul, not a spontaneous flash of insight. This lesson unpacks each element of his definition and addresses a subtle objection about the temporal process of reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the commentary is dense and technical.
2
Identify the components of the definition
Look for the three parts: observation with intention, the specific meaning of 'mind', and the purpose of obtaining something else. Understand how each part narrows the scope of nazar.
The definition excludes intuition (hads) and presential knowledge.
3
Understand the exclusion of intuition
Intuition (hads) is excluded because it is a spontaneous arrangement of principles without intentional seeking. Nazar requires intention and choice.
This is a key distinction in Islamic epistemology.
4
Analyze the objection about motions
The commentator addresses an objection: observation during a single step (first motion) does not yield the sought result. The response is that nazar is the continuous process from first to second motion.
There is a subtle nuance about an individual instance of observation during the first motion being a part of the whole.
5
Reflect on the nuance
The commentator notes that an individual observation during the first motion can be an instance of nazar when considered in the definition of a single object. This subtlety has been missed by some scholars.
This shows the depth of kalam analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (هو ملاحظة العقل إلخ) أي بقصد واختيار كما هو المتبادر ، فخرج الحدس إذ هو المبادي المرتبة من غير طلب والعقل وإن كان يطلق على التعقل والنفس الناطقة والقوة العاقلة، والجوهر المجرد إلا أن المراد منه النفس الناطقة بقرينة أن الملاحظة فعلها، وإن المجردات علمها حضوري لا حصولي، ثم الملاحظة لأجل تحصيل الغير يقتضي أن يكون ذلك التحصيل غاية مترتبة عليه في الجملة فلا يرد النقض بالملاحظة التي عند الحركة الأولى، والثانية إذ لا يترتب عليها التحصيل أصلاً، بل إنما يترتب على الملاحظة التي هي من ابتداء الحركة الأولى إلى انتهاء الحركة الثانية، نعم يترتب على الملاحظة التي بالحركة الأولى في التعريف بالمفرد، وهي فرد منه فتدبر، وإنه مع ظهوره قد خفي على بعض .
His statement: 'it is the mind's observation etc.' i.e., with intention and choice as is apparent, so intuition (al-hads) is excluded for it is the arrangement of principles without seeking. And 'mind' (al-'aql), although it is applied to intellection, the rational soul, the rational faculty, and the abstract substance, what is meant here is the rational soul by the evidence that observation is its act, and the abstract things have presential knowledge not acquired. Then observation for the sake of obtaining something else requires that the obtaining be a goal consequent upon it in general, so the objection by the observation during the first and second motion does not apply, for obtaining does not result from it at all; rather it results from the observation from the beginning of the first motion to the end of the second motion. Yes, it results from the observation during the first motion in the definition of the individual, and it is an individual of it, so reflect, and although it is apparent, it has been hidden from some.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of nazar as 'the mind's observation of what it possesses to obtain something else.' The commentator explains each element. First, 'observation' (mulahaza) is intentional and voluntary, so intuition (hads) is excluded because intuition spontaneously perceives implications without seeking. Second, 'mind' (al-'aql) here specifically means the rational soul (al-nafs al-natiqa), not other meanings, because observation is an act of the soul. The knowledge of abstract substances is presential (huduri), not acquired (husuli), so they are not subjects of this definition. Third, the phrase 'to obtain something else' implies that the obtaining is a goal that follows from the observation. This allows two potential objections: (1) Observation during a single step (e.g., first motion of reasoning) does not by itself yield the sought; the commentator responds that the observation considered is the continuous process from the first motion to the second motion. (2) There is a nuance that an individual instance of observation during the first motion can be considered as a part of the entire process, and it is an individual of nazar. The commentator emphasizes that this is subtle and has eluded some scholars.

العلم الحصولي
الحركة الأولى
الحركة الثانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: nazar includes any mental act, including spontaneous intuition
Correction: nazar specifically requires intention and choice, excluding intuition (hads) and presential knowledge

Checkpoint

A) Certain knowledge.
B) Presential knowledge.
C) Intuition (hads).
D) Probable opinion.

Key Takeaway
Nazar is the deliberate, intentional observation by the rational soul of known premises to arrive at a new conclusion, distinct from spontaneous intuition.

Nazar as Mere Attention: The Emanationist View
Sanusi reports and critiques the view that nazar is mere attention based on emanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative view that nazar is mere attention based on universal emanation, its two definitions (privational and existential), and the critique of this view.

Core Idea
Some philosophers consider nazar as mere attention, relying on emanation.

Sanusi now examines a radically different account of nazar. Instead of requiring prior information and logical steps, some philosophers—drawing on the doctrine of universal emanation—argue that simply directing one's attention to the sought object suffices for it to be received. This view comes in two versions: privational and existential. In this passage, Sanusi reports this view and responds to objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking, noting the two definitions and the subsequent discussion.
OCR risk is high, so expect possible scribal errors.
2
Identify the emanationist basis
Notice that the view is grounded in the doctrine of universal emanation: when we direct attention, the sought flows from the source without prior information.
3
Distinguish the two definitions
The passage presents a privational definition (abstraction from distractions) and an existential definition (focusing the mind on intelligibles). Note the analogy to sight.
4
Follow the objection and reply
An objection is raised: using prior information is natural, so how can they deny it? The reply is that bringing information to mind is itself a form of attention that cuts off distractions.
5
Examine the final criticisms
The passage ends with several objections to the definition of nazar as 'mind's observation of what it possesses to obtain something else', and responses to some of them.

وأهل الرياضة .

قوله : ( ما هو حاصل عنده لتحصيل غيره أي من ذلك الحاصل كما هو المتبادر، فلا يرد عليه أن التعريف المذكور يصدق على ملاحظة إحدى مقدمتي الدليل مثلاً، مع أنه ليس بنظر، وذلك لأن ملاحظة الصغرى مثلاً ليس لتحصيل المطلوب منها ، بل لينضم إليها الكبرى، ويحصل المطلوب من المجموع وقد يناقش في التعريف المذكور بأن ملاحظة العقل مشترك، وبأنه يختص بالحركة الأولى والكفر مجموع الحركتين، وبأن الملاحظة بعد وجدان المبادي المناسبة لتحصيل ما هو أنسب منها عند عدم حصوله يصدق عليه التعريف، وليس من النظر وأيضاً خروج الحدس منه غير ظاهر والجواب عن الأول منع اشتراكه عند المتكلمين ولو سلم، فالقرينة معينة
As for those who consider it (i.e., nazar) mere attention, based on the principle that the source is universal emanation, such that when we direct ourselves to that sought, it emanates upon us without us having assistance from prior information. Among them some made it privational and said: it is the mind's abstraction from distractions that prevent obtaining the sought. And among them some made it existential and said: it is the mind's focusing on intelligibles, and they likened it to the focusing of sight on visible objects. And it may be said: just as perception by sight depends... [unclear]. His statement 'without problem, unlike the previous, for it has two problems that need to be resolved'... His statement: 'without there being' etc. If you say: assistance is self-evident, so how can they deny it? I say: perhaps he says that bringing to mind information is one way of attention, for it helps cut off attention to other than the sought, and so the sought may be obtained by mere attention without prior information, as is the way of the Indian sages and the practitioners of spiritual exercise. His statement: 'toward the intelligibles' i.e., the sought matters, as indicated by their likening to visible objects, and his explicit statement in what may be said: where he said: 'and the mind's focusing on it' so the intended by 'intelligibles' is what is liable to become intelligible, and the choice of plural form to specify inclusion of conceptual and assertive sought matters, certain and others, although the singular form would have been apparent. His statement: 'what is present with him to obtain something else' i.e., from that present as is apparent, so the objection that the mentioned definition applies to the observation of one of the two premises of a proof, for example, while it is not nazar, is not valid, because the observation of the minor premise, for instance, is not to obtain the sought from it, but rather to join it with the major premise, and the sought is obtained from the whole. And one may discuss the mentioned definition: that 'mind's observation' is ambiguous, and that it is specific to the first motion, and the [؟] is the sum of two motions, and that observation after finding appropriate principles to obtain what is more appropriate when it is not obtained applies to the definition, but is not nazar, and also the exclusion of intuition from it is not apparent. The answer to the first is the denial that it is ambiguous among the theologians, and if granted, the context specifies.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents alternative views on nazar. Some philosophers consider it mere attention (tawajjuh) based on the doctrine of universal emanation (al-fayd): when we direct ourselves to the sought, it flows from the active source without needing prior information. These philosophers divide into two groups: one makes it privational ('adami), defining it as the mind's abstraction from distractions; the other makes it existential (wujudi), defining it as the mind's focusing on intelligibles, similar to how the eye focuses on visible objects. The commentator then addresses an objection: since using prior information is natural, how can they deny it? He replies that bringing information to mind is itself a form of attention that cuts off distractions, so the sought can be obtained by mere attention, as practiced by Indian sages and ascetics. Then the commentator clarifies terms: 'toward the intelligibles' means 'toward the sought matters' (al-matalib), and the plural form indicates inclusion of all types: conceptual and assertive, certain and non-certain. Finally, he discusses objections to the definition of nazar as 'the mind's observation of what it possesses to obtain something else': (1) it could apply to observing a single premise, which is not nazar, but the reply is that the observation is for joining premises, not for obtaining the sought from that premise alone. (2) 'Mind's observation' is ambiguous, but among theologians it is not ambiguous; if it were, context clarifies. (3) It is specific to the first motion while nazar includes both motions, but this is addressed. (4) Intuition is not clearly excluded, but the answer is not fully given here.

حكماء الهند

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nazar always requires prior information and logical deduction.
Correction: Some philosophers argue that nazar can be mere attention, relying on emanation, without prior premises.

Checkpoint
According to the view based on universal emanation, how does nazar work?
A) By deducing from known premises.
B) By simply directing attention to the sought, which then emanates.
C) By abstracting the mind from all thoughts.
D) By focusing the eyes on the text.

Key Takeaway
The alternative view of nazar as mere attention is based on the doctrine of universal emanation and has privational and existential definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your pursuit of religious truth, do you lean more toward systematic reasoning or toward a quiet, attentive openness? Which approach has led to deeper certainty for you, and why? Reflect on the emanationist claim that truth can come through mere attention—does this resonate with any moment of sudden insight you've experienced? How do you balance trust in God's gift of knowledge with the effort of disciplined inquiry?
?
The passage describes prior information as a means of cutting off distractions rather than as a source of premises. Think of your own study of theology: have you ever used proofs or texts merely to quiet your mind and focus on God? Or have they become a distraction themselves? What would it take for you to practice 'tajrid'—abstraction from mental clutter—in your pursuit of understanding?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate

The Definition of Nazar and Its Core Elements
Defines nazar and clarifies its boundaries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of nazar as the mind's intentional observation to acquire something else, its exclusion of intuition, and the role of the first and second motions.

Core Idea
Nazar is the mind's deliberate observation to acquire unknown knowledge, excluding spontaneous intuition.

This lesson introduces the foundational definition of nazar (theoretical reasoning) as articulated by al-Sharif al-Jurjani. The definition emphasizes intentionality and the goal of acquisition, distinguishing it from intuition and clarifying the process of reasoning through successive motions. By understanding this definition, you will grasp the essential nature of nazar as a deliberate intellectual act.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Note the key phrase 'ملاحظة العقل ما هو حاصل عنده لتحصيل غيره'.
This phrase is the core definition.
2
Understand the exclusion of intuition (hads)
The commentator explicitly excludes intuition because it is not intentional. Compare nazar's deliberate nature with the spontaneity of intuition.
Intuition is a sudden attainment without seeking.
3
Clarify the meaning of 'mind' (al-ʿaql)
Here 'mind' refers to the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa), not other meanings. Observation is its act, and immaterial substances have presential knowledge, not acquired.
This avoids ambiguity.
4
Examine the motion clarification
Nazar is not just the first motion or the second motion alone; it is the continuous observation from the beginning of the first motion to the end of the second motion. The observation at the first motion alone is an instance for simple definitions.
This resolves a potential counterexample.
5
Reflect on the definition's significance
Why is this definition important for kalam? It sets the stage for all theological reasoning, emphasizing intentional pursuit of knowledge.
This connects to the broader study.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملاحظة العقل ما هو حاصل عنده لتحصيل غيره هذا قوله: (هو ملاحظة العقل إلخ) أي بقصد واختيار كما هو المتبادر، فخرج الحدس إذ هو المبادي المرتبة من غير طلب والعقل وإن كان يطلق على التعقل والنفس الناطقة والقوة العاقلة، والجوهر المجرد إلا أن المراد منه النفس الناطقة بقرينة أن الملاحظة فعلها، وإن المجردات علمها حضوري لا حصولي، ثم الملاحظة لأجل تحصيل الغير يقتضي أن يكون ذلك التحصيل غاية مترتبة عليه في الجملة فلا يرد النقض بالملاحظة التي عند الحركة الأولى، والثانية إذ لا يترتب عليها التحصيل أصلاً، بل إنما يترتب على الملاحظة التي هي من ابتداء الحركة الأولى إلى انتهاء الحركة الثانية، نعم يترتب على الملاحظة التي بالحركة الأولى في التعريف بالمفرد، وهي فرد منه فتدبر، وإنه مع ظهوره قد خفي على بعض . قوله: (من غير أن يكون إلخ فإن قلت الاستعانة بديهية فكيف ينكرها؟ قلت لعله يقول : إن إحضار المعلومات طريق من طرق التوجه فإنه يفيد قطع الالتفات إلى غير المطلوب ولذا قد يحصل المطلوب بمجرد التوجه بدون معلومات سابقة على ماهو طريقة حكماء الهند وأهل الرياضة . قوله: (نحو المعقولات أي المطالب كما يدل عليه تشبيهها بالمبصرات، وتصريحه فيما قد يقال : حيث قال : وتحديق العقل نحوه فالمراد بالمعقولات ما من شأنه أن يصير معقولات واختيار صيغة الجمع للتنصيص بشموله للمطالب التصورية والتصديقية اليقينية وغيرها وإن كان الظاهر صيغة المفرد . قوله: (ما هو حاصل عنده لتحصيل غيره أي من ذلك الحاصل كما هو المتبادر، فلا يرد عليه أن التعريف المذكور يصدق على ملاحظة إحدى مقدمتي الدليل مثلاً، مع أنه ليس بنظر، وذلك لأن ملاحظة الصغرى مثلاً ليس لتحصيل المطلوب منها، بل لينضم إليها الكبرى، ويحصل المطلوب من المجموع وقد يناقش في التعريف المذكور بأن ملاحظة العقل مشترك، وبأنه يختص بالحركة الأولى والكفر مجموع الحركتين، وبأن الملاحظة بعد وجدان المبادي المناسبة لتحصيل ما هو أنسب منها عند عدم حصوله يصدق عليه التعريف، وليس من النظر وأيضاً خروج الحدس منه غير ظاهر والجواب عن الأول منع اشتراكه عند المتكلمين ولو سلم، فالقرينة معينة، وعن الثاني منع اختصاصها بها، فإن في الترتيب ملاحظة للمرتب على وجه مخصوص، وعن الثالث ما أشرنا إليه في توجيه قوله : لتحصيل غيره وعن الرابع وضوح القرينة على أن المراد لتحصيل الغير بطريق الاكتساب .
The mind's observation of what is present with it to acquire something else. His statement: (it is the mind's observation, etc.) i.e., with intent and choice as is evident, so intuition (hads) is excluded, for it is the arranged principles without seeking. And the mind (al-ʿaql), although it is applied to intellection, the rational soul, the rational faculty, and the immaterial substance, yet what is meant by it here is the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa) by the evidence that observation is its act, and that immaterial substances have presential knowledge, not acquired. Then, observation for the sake of acquiring something else requires that acquisition be a goal consequent upon it in general, so the objection does not apply to the observation at the first motion and the second, since no acquisition results from them at all; rather, acquisition results from the observation that is from the beginning of the first motion to the end of the second motion. Yes, it results from the observation at the first motion in the definition by a simple term, and it is an instance of it, so reflect, and although it is apparent, it has been hidden from some. His statement: (without being, etc.). If you say: reliance on known things is self-evident, how can he deny it? I say: perhaps he says that bringing to mind known things is one of the ways of attention, for it helps to cut off attention from other than the sought object, and so sometimes the sought object is obtained by mere attention without prior known things, as is the way of the sages of India and the practitioners of spiritual exercises. His statement: (toward intelligibles i.e., the sought objects, as indicated by his comparing them to visible things, and his explicit statement in what may be said: where he said: and the mind's focusing toward it. So what is meant by intelligibles is what is capable of becoming intelligible, and his choice of the plural form is to explicitly indicate its inclusion of conceptual and assertive sought objects, certain and otherwise, although the singular form would be apparent. His statement: (what is present with him to acquire something else) i.e., from that present thing, as is evident, so the objection does not apply that the mentioned definition applies to the observation of one of the two premises of a proof, for example, while it is not reasoning, because the observation of the minor premise, for example, is not for acquiring the sought object from it, but rather to join it with the major premise, and the sought object is obtained from the whole. And the mentioned definition may be objected to on the grounds that the mind's observation is common, and that it is specific to the first motion and that the two motions together, and that observation after finding appropriate principles to acquire what is more appropriate when it is not obtained applies to the definition, and it is not reasoning, and also the exclusion of intuition from it is not apparent. The answer to the first is: the denial of its commonness among the theologians (mutakallimūn), and even if it is granted, the context specifies it. And to the second: the denial of its exclusivity to it, for in the arrangement there is an observation of the arranged thing in a specific manner. And to the third: what we indicated in his statement: to acquire something else. And to the fourth: the clarity of the context that what is meant is to acquire something else by way of acquisition.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage defines nazar (theoretical reasoning) and then defends it against four objections. The passage begins with the definition: 'The mind's observation of what is present with it to acquire something else.' The commentator explains that this observation must be intentional and voluntary, thereby excluding intuition (hads), which is spontaneous. He clarifies that 'mind' here refers to the rational soul, as observation is its act, and immaterial substances have presential knowledge, not acquired. He then addresses a potential counterexample: observation at the first or second motion of reasoning does not yield acquisition; only the continuous observation from the beginning of the first motion to the end of the second does. However, observation at the first motion alone can define a simple term, and that is a valid instance. After explaining the various phrases of the definition, he presents four objections: (1) that 'mind's observation' is ambiguous, (2) that the definition applies only to the first motion, (3) that observation after finding appropriate principles applies to the definition but is not reasoning, and (4) that intuition is not clearly excluded. He answers each: (1) theologians restrict the term, and context clarifies it; (2) the definition does not exclude the second motion because there is observation of the arranged thing in a specific order; (3) as already indicated in the phrase 'to acquire something else'; (4) the context shows that acquisition is meant by way of acquisition, not intuition.

Misconception: Some think nazar includes any mental observation, including spontaneous intuition or any observation of the mind.
Correction: Nazar must be intentional (with intent and choice) and aimed at acquisition; intuition lacks intentional seeking and is therefore excluded.

What does the definition of nazar explicitly exclude?

Key Takeaway
Nazar is the mind's deliberate, purpose-driven observation to acquire unknown knowledge, distinct from spontaneous intuition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

When was the last time you deliberately examined a belief you held, step by step, rather than accepting it impulsively? Commit to one area of your faith where you will practice nazar this week—moving from the first motion to the second until you truly acquire understanding.
?
The author warns that not all mental observations qualify as nazar—only those with purpose and choice. How much of your daily thinking is passive, reactive, or driven by intuition? If nazar is required for true knowledge, what do you need to change about your approach to learning Islam?
?
Consider the 'motion' of your own reasoning. Do you often stop after the first observation, satisfied with a surface understanding? The passage says acquisition requires continuity from first to second motion. Where in your religious practice have you stopped short of deeper comprehension, and how will you push to the second motion starting today?

What Is Kalam?

Defending Nazar: Four Objections and Their Answers
Presents and refutes four objections to the definition of nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four objections raised against the definition of nazar and understand al-Jurjani's responses to each.

Core Idea
The definition of nazar withstands scrutiny: objections about ambiguity, exclusivity, and intuition are all answered.

Having established the definition of nazar, al-Jurjani now addresses potential criticisms. He presents four objections concerning the commonality of the term, the scope of motions, post-discovery observation, and the exclusion of intuition. His careful responses reinforce the definition's precision and necessity for theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections and answers
Read the Arabic passage and translation from the start of the objections to the end. Note the structure: each objection is followed by an answer.
The objections are explicitly numbered in the commentary.
2
Identify the first objection and answer
The first objection is that 'mind's observation' is a common term. The answer: theologians restrict its meaning, and context specifies it.
This clarifies ambiguity.
3
Identify the second and third objections and answers
Second objection: nazar is specific to the first motion only. Answer: the definition does not exclude the second motion because the arrangement involves observation of the arranged thing. Third objection: observation after finding appropriate principles fits the definition but is not true nazar. Answer: it is not for acquisition of that particular thing; it's already acquired.
These objections challenge the scope of the definition.
4
Examine the fourth objection and answer
Fourth objection: the exclusion of intuition is not apparent. Answer: the context shows that acquisition is meant by way of acquisition (iktiṣāb), not intuition.
This reinforces the intentional nature.
5
Reflect on the importance of these objections
Why does the author defend the definition so thoroughly? It shows that nazar is a technical term that must be precise for theological argumentation.
This connects to the broader methodology of kalam.

Commentary
This passage defines nazar (theoretical reasoning) and then defends it against four objections. The passage begins with the definition: 'The mind's observation of what is present with it to acquire something else.' The commentator explains that this observation must be intentional and voluntary, thereby excluding intuition (hads), which is spontaneous. He clarifies that 'mind' here refers to the rational soul, as observation is its act, and immaterial substances have presential knowledge, not acquired. He then addresses a potential counterexample: observation at the first or second motion of reasoning does not yield acquisition; only the continuous observation from the beginning of the first motion to the end of the second does. However, observation at the first motion alone can define a simple term, and that is a valid instance. After explaining the various phrases of the definition, he presents four objections: (1) that 'mind's observation' is ambiguous, (2) that the definition applies only to the first motion, (3) that observation after finding appropriate principles applies to the definition but is not reasoning, and (4) that intuition is not clearly excluded. He answers each: (1) theologians restrict the term, and context clarifies it; (2) the definition does not exclude the second motion because there is observation of the arranged thing in a specific order; (3) as already indicated in the phrase 'to acquire something else'; (4) the context shows that acquisition is meant by way of acquisition, not intuition.

Key Terms
نظر
ملاحظة العقل
حدس
اكتساب
الحركة الأولى
الحركة الثانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the objections are unanswerable or that the definition is flawed.
Correction: The objections are systematically refuted, showing the definition's precision and robustness.

Checkpoint
What is the answer to the objection that the definition applies to observation after finding appropriate principles?
A) The observation is not intentional
B) The phrase 'to acquire something else' indicates it is for acquisition of something not yet present
C) The principles are not appropriate
D) The observation is not of the mind

Key Takeaway
The definition of nazar is defended against four objections, confirming its precision and necessity for theological reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which area of your religious life are you most tempted to rely on 'mere attention' or intuition rather than building your beliefs on careful premises? What is one specific truth you will actively verify today through acquisition (iktisāb) rather than assume?
?
When you hold a belief about Allah or His attributes, can you clearly articulate the logical steps that lead to that conclusion—or do you find yourself saying 'it just feels right'? Will you commit to studying the proofs behind one core belief this week?
?
The passage defends razor-sharp definitions against objections. Is there a theological concept you believe you understand but cannot define precisely? How might that ambiguity be allowing error to enter your faith without you noticing?

Three Alternative Definitions of Nazar
Al-Jurjani presents three alternative definitions of nazar and an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three alternative definitions of nazar (reasoning) and the analogy between sight and insight.

Core Idea
Reasoning is not just attention; it requires active mental focus and purification.

Before exploring the full account of reasoning, al-Jurjani presents several alternative views. These definitions—mere attention, privative purification, and existential focusing—set the stage for a deeper discussion. The author also draws a powerful analogy between physical sight and mental insight to clarify the necessary conditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the alternative definitions
Read the Arabic passage about those who consider nazar as mere attention, and the two sub-views (privative and existential). Notice the key terms: التوجه, تجريد الذهن, تحديق العقل.
These definitions are based on emanationist and philosophical assumptions.
2
Understand the analogy with sight
The author compares visual perception (requiring facing, turning the pupil, removing the veil) to intellectual insight (attention, focusing, purification). Reflect on how this makes the definition more concrete.
The analogy is a common pedagogical tool in kalam.
3
Identify the three conditions for insight
Identify the three necessary conditions for intellectual perception mentioned in the analogy: attention, focusing, and purification.

قوله : ( وأما من يراه ) أي النظر ( مجرد التوجه ) إلى المطلوب الإدراكي بناء على أن المبدأ عام الفيض، فمتى توجهنا إلى ذلك المطلوب أفاضه علينا من غير أن يكون لنا في ذلك استعانة بمعلومات سابقة (فمنهم من بالبصر يتوقف على أمور ثلاثة مواجهة المبصر وتقليب الحدقة نحوه طلباً لرؤيته، وإزالة الغشاوة من الإبصار، كذلك الإدراك بالبصيرة يتوقف على أمور ثلاثة التوجه نحو المطلوب، وتحديق العقل نحوه طلباً لإدراكه وتجريد العقل عن الغفلات التي هي بمنزلة الغشاوة، واعلم أن الظاهر مذهب أصحاب التعاليم وهو أن النظر اكتساب المجهولات من المعلومات وحينئذ نقول : لا شبهة في أن كل مجهول لا يمكن اكتسابه من أي معلوم اتفق بل لا بد له من معلومات مناسبة إياه، ولا شك أيضاً في أنه لا يمكن تحصيله من تلك المعلومات على أي وجه كانت، بل لا بد هناك من ترتیب معين فيما بينها ومن هيئة مخصوصة عارضة لها بسبب ذلك الترتيب، فإذا حصل لنا شعور ما بأمر تصوري أو تصديقي وحاولنا تحصيله على وجه أكمل، فلا بد أن يتحرك الذهن في المعلومات المخزونة عنده منتقلا من معلوم إلى آخر حتى يجد قوله : ( التوجه نحو المطلوب) أي في الجملة بحيث يمتاز المطلوب عما عداه كما يمتاز المبصر بمواجهة البصر عن غيره . قوله : ( وتحديق العقل إلخ) أي التوجه التام إليه بحيث يشغله عما سواه كتقليب الحدقة إلى المبصر . قوله : ( واعلم إلخ ) تحقيق للمقام بحيث يتجلى الحق ويرفع النزاع. قوله : ( إن الظاهر مذهب إلخ لما مر من أن الاستعانة بالمعلومات أمر بديهي، كيف لا وتختلف النتائج بحسب اختلافها إيجابا وسلباً وقوة وضعفاً . تقديم قوله : (من معلومات مخصوصة كالذاتيات في الحدود واللوازم البينة الشاملة في الرسوم والحدود الوسطى في الاقترانيات وقضية الملازمة في الشرطيات . قوله : ( ومن هيئة (مخصوصة لا يختلجن في وهمك أن هذا القول : يقتضي أن يكون الجنس على الفصل في المعرفات واجباً ليحصل به الهيئة المخصوصة مع أن ذلك ليس بلازم عند أهل التحقيق فإن المراد من الهيئة المخصوصة فيها في الهيئة الحاصلة من انضمام أحدهما إلى الآخر، لتحصل صورة وحدانية مطابقة للمعرف سواء قدم الجنس أو الفصل. قوله : (لو حاولنا تحصيله إلخ) أي تحصيل ذلك الأمر على وجه أكمل من الوجه السابق، سواء قلنا : أن ذلك الوجه هو المطلوب، أو أن المطلوب ذلك الأمر بهذا الوجه على ما حققناه في جواب الشبهة الأولى للإمام في امتناع اكتساب التصور، وقد عرفت هناك بيان كونه أكمل من الوجه السابق فارجع إليه .
His statement: (But as for those who consider it) i.e., reasoning (mere attention) to the cognitive sought object, based on the principle that the Source is universally effusive, so whenever we attend to that sought object, He pours it upon us without our having any assistance from prior known things. (Some of them made it privative and said: it is the purification of the mind from distractions) that prevent the attainment of the sought object. (And some of them made it existential and said: it is the focusing of the mind toward intelligibles) and they likened it to the focusing of sight (toward visible things). And it may be said: just as perception by sight depends on three things: facing the visible object, turning the pupil toward it seeking to see it, and removing the veil from sight, likewise perception by insight depends on three things: attention toward the sought object, focusing the mind toward it seeking to perceive it, and purifying the mind from distractions which are like a veil. And know that the apparent position of the People of Instructions (aṣḥāb al-taʿālīm) is that reasoning is the acquisition of unknowns from knowns. Here we say: there is no doubt that every unknown cannot be acquired from any known at random; rather, it must have appropriate knowns for it. And there is also no doubt that it cannot be obtained from those knowns in any manner whatsoever; rather, there must be a specific arrangement among them and a specific form incidental to them due to that arrangement. So if we have some awareness of a conceptual or assertive matter and we attempt to acquire it in a more complete manner, then the mind must move among the stored knowns, transitioning from one known to another until it finds. His statement: (attention toward the sought object) i.e., in general, such that the sought object is distinguished from others, just as the visible object is distinguished from others by facing the sight. His statement: (and focusing the mind, etc.) i.e., complete attention to it such that it occupies it from other things, like turning the pupil toward the visible object. His statement: (and know, etc.) is a verification of the matter such that the truth becomes clear and the dispute is removed. His statement: (for the apparent is the position, etc.) because of what has passed that reliance on known things is a self-evident matter; how could it not be, while results differ according to their difference positively and negatively and in strength and weakness? He presents: His statement: (from specific knowns, such as essential properties in definitions, common necessary concomitants in descriptions, middle terms in conjunctive syllogisms, and the proposition of implication in conditional syllogisms. His statement: (and from a specific form) – do not let it occur to your mind that this statement requires that the genus be prior to the differentia in definientia necessarily so that the specific form is obtained, whereas that is not necessary according to the people of investigation, for what is meant by the specific form in them is the form resulting from joining one to the other, so that a unitary image conforming to the definiendum is obtained, whether the genus is advanced or the differentia. His statement: (If we attempt to acquire it, etc.) i.e., to acquire that matter in a more complete manner than the previous manner, whether we say that that manner is the sought object, or that the sought object is that matter in this manner, as we have verified in the answer to the first objection of the Imam regarding the impossibility of acquiring a concept, and you have known there the explanation of it being more complete than the previous manner, so refer to it.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents three alternative definitions of nazar (reasoning) and then elaborates the view of the People of Instructions (أصحاب التعاليم). The author begins by stating that some define reasoning as mere attention to the cognitive sought object, based on the emanationist principle that the First Principle bestows knowledge when we attend. This view is divided into a privative definition (purifying the mind from distractions) and an existential definition (focusing the mind toward intelligibles), likened to focusing sight on visible objects. The author then presents an analogy: just as visual perception requires three things (facing the object, turning the pupil, removing the veil), so insight requires three things: attention toward the sought object, focusing the mind, and purifying the mind from distractions. He then states that the apparent position of the People of Instructions (likely the philosophers) is that reasoning is the acquisition of unknowns from knowns. He elaborates that an unknown cannot be acquired from any known; it requires appropriate knowns and a specific arrangement among them with a specific form. The mind moves among stored knowns until it finds what is needed. He then comments on the three conditions: attention in general to distinguish the sought object, complete focus that occupies the mind, and the verification that reliance on knowns is self-evident. He specifies the types of knowns: essential properties in definitions, necessary concomitants in descriptions, middle terms in syllogisms, and the proposition of implication in conditionals. He clarifies that the specific form does not require genus to precede differentia; rather, the form results from their combination regardless of order. Finally, he notes that when attempting to acquire a matter more completely, the aim is either the manner itself or the matter in that manner, as discussed in the answer to the first objection against the acquisition of concepts.

التوجه
تحديق العقل
تجريد الذهن

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that reasoning is effortless attendance or mere removal of obstacles.
Correction: The passage shows that reasoning requires both active focusing (existential) and removal of distractions (privative), analogous to physical sight.

Checkpoint
According to the analogy in the passage, how many conditions are required for insight (الإدراك بالبصيرة)?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Nazar (reasoning) can be defined as attention, purification, or focusing, and the analogy with sight shows it requires all three aspects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sit to study or reflect, do you genuinely purify your mind from distractions—like turning off notifications and quieting inner chatter—or do you settle for mere attention, assuming the truth will pour in on its own? What would change if you took the 'removing the veil' step seriously today?
?
The analogy compares focusing the mind to turning the pupil toward an object. Are you actively 'turning your mental pupil' toward the truths you seek, or do you passively wait for understanding to come? What is one specific truth you have been neglecting to focus on?
?
The passage implies that your own mental effort—both clearing distractions and focusing—is necessary, not optional. Do you habitually rely on external sources (teachers, books, lectures) without doing the inner work of tajrid and tahdiq? What is one distraction you can commit to eliminating this week to sharpen your spiritual sight?
