Nazar as Acquisition of Unknowns from Knowns
Al-Jurjani states and elaborates the view of the People of Instructions on nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the view of the People of Instructions that reasoning is the acquisition of unknowns from knowns, and the necessary conditions for this process.

Core Idea
Reasoning is a structured acquisition: unknowns are deduced from appropriate knowns with a specific arrangement.

After surveying alternative definitions, al-Jurjani turns to what he considers the apparent position of the People of Instructions (likely philosophers): reasoning as acquiring unknowns from knowns. He emphasizes that this process is not random but requires suitable knowns, a specific order, and a resulting form. The passage then details the types of knowns used and clarifies potential misunderstandings.

Guided Path
1
Read the core definition
Read the statement: 'واعلم أن الظاهر مذهب أصحاب التعاليم وهو أن النظر اكتساب المجهولات من المعلومات'. Understand that reasoning is an active acquisition, not passive reception.
The term 'الظاهر' indicates this is the most evident view.
2
Identify the conditions
The passage states that unknowns cannot be acquired from any known; they require appropriate knowns, a specific arrangement (ترتیب معين), and a specific form (هيئة مخصوصة). Find where these are mentioned.
3
Examine the types of knowns
The author lists specific knowns used in definitions and syllogisms: essential properties, necessary concomitants, middle terms, and implications. Note the Arabic terms.
This shows the range of knowns needed for different types of reasoning.
4
Clarify the specific form
Read the comment about the specific form not requiring genus prior to differentia. Understand that the form results from the combination of elements regardless of order.
This prevents a potential misinterpretation about necessary order.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

The Three Conditions for Inner Perception and the Sight Analogy
The author establishes the analogy between sight and inner perception with three conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three conditions required for inner intellectual perception and how they parallel the conditions for physical sight.

Core Idea
Inner perception requires direction, focus, and clarity — just like seeing.

How do we come to know something through inner reflection? Drawing on a powerful analogy with physical sight, the author explains that inner perception depends on three essential conditions. By understanding these, we can better appreciate the process of nazar (contemplation) that leads to knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the parallel structure between the conditions for sight and inner perception.
2
Identify the three conditions for sight
The author lists three conditions for physical sight: facing the object, turning the pupil, and removing the veil. Make sure you understand each.
These are straightforward physical actions.
3
Identify the three conditions for inner perception
Now find the three corresponding conditions for inner perception: directing towards the sought, focusing the intellect, and stripping distractions.
Notice the terms 'tawajjuh', 'tahdeeq', and 'tajreed'.
4
Understand the commentary's clarifications
The commentary explains that 'directing' means distinguishing the object from others, and 'focusing' means complete attention that occupies the mind entirely.
The third condition is implied: removing distractions.
5
Reflect on the analogy's significance
Consider why the author uses this analogy. It shows that inner perception is an active process requiring intentional effort, not passive reception.
This prepares for the definition of nazar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أمور ثلاثة مواجهة المبصر وتقليب الحدقة نحوه طلباً لرؤيته، وإزالة الغشاوة من الإبصار، كذلك الإدراك بالبصيرة يتوقف على أمور ثلاثة التوجه نحو المطلوب، وتحديق العقل نحوه طلباً لإدراكه وتجريد العقل عن الغفلات التي هي بمنزلة الغشاوة. قوله : ( التوجه نحو المطلوب) أي في الجملة بحيث يمتاز المطلوب عما عداه كما يمتاز المبصر بمواجهة البصر عن غيره . قوله : ( وتحديق العقل إلخ) أي التوجه التام إليه بحيث يشغله عما سواه كتقليب الحدقة إلى المبصر .
Upon three things: facing the seen object and turning the pupil towards it seeking to see it, and removing the veil from sight. Likewise, perception by the inner sight depends on three things: directing towards the sought, and focusing the intellect towards it seeking to grasp it, and stripping the intellect of distractions which are like the veil. His saying: (Directing towards the sought) meaning in general such that the sought is distinguished from what is other than it just as the seen object is distinguished by facing the sight from other than it. His saying: (And focusing the intellect etc.) meaning complete attention to it such that it occupies it from other than it, like turning the pupil towards the seen object.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage draws an analogy between physical sight and intellectual inner perception (idrak bil-basira). It first lists three conditions for sight: facing the object, turning the pupil towards it, and removing any obstruction (ghashawa). Then it states that inner perception likewise depends on three conditions: directing (tawajjuh) towards the sought (matlub), focusing (tahdeeq) the intellect towards it, and stripping (tajreed) the intellect of distractions (ghaflat). The commentary clarifies that 'directing towards the sought' means distinguishing it from other things, just as the eye faces the seen object. 'Focusing the intellect' means complete attention that occupies the mind entirely, akin to turning the pupil. The third condition is implied to be the removal of mental distractions. This sets the stage for defining nazar.

بصيرة
توجه
تحدق
غفلات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Inner perception requires no preparation or active effort.
Correction: The analogy shows that inner perception requires active direction, focus, and removal of distractions, just as sight requires facing, turning, and clarity.

Checkpoint
According to the analogy, how many conditions are required for inner perception?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
Inner perception is not passive; it requires active direction, intense focus, and mental clarity — all of which can be cultivated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sit to learn or reflect, do you truly 'direct yourself' (tawajjuh) to the meaning, or are your thoughts scattered? What is one thing you can do right now to turn your heart toward truth?
?
Which distraction (ghafla) is currently veiling your intellect from understanding a deep truth you know you need? Would you be willing to name it and take a practical step to remove it today?
?
This lesson says inner perception requires intense focus that occupies you entirely. In your pursuit of knowledge, do you give it such focus, or is your mind divided? What would change if you devoted full attention to understanding one verse or concept?

The Definition of Nazar and Its Prerequisites
Definition of nazar with conditions for acquisition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why speculative reasoning (nazar) requires not only appropriate knowns but also a specific arrangement and form to acquire unknowns.

Core Idea
Nazar is the methodical acquisition of the unknown from the appropriate known.

The author defines speculative reasoning (nazar) as the acquisition of unknowns from knowns—but immediately clarifies that not just any known will do. He carefully lists the kinds of knowns that are suitable, and then explains that even these need a specific order and structure to yield knowledge. This lesson unpacks that definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its structure. Then read the translation to understand the meaning. Notice the flow: definition, first condition, second condition, examples, and then responses to objections.
The passage is dense but logically organized.
2
Identify the key conditions
Find the two requirements for acquiring an unknown: (1) appropriate knowns, and (2) a specific arrangement and specific form. Understand why both are necessary.
The author emphasizes that not any known works, nor any order.
3
Review the examples and objections
Look at the examples of specific knowns in different logical contexts (definitions, descriptions, syllogisms). Then see how the author responds to a potential misunderstanding about the order of genus and species.
The objections clarify that the specific form is just the union of components, regardless of order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الظاهر مذهب أصحاب التعليمات وهو أن النظر اكتساب المجهولات من المعلومات وحينئذ نقول : لا شبهة في أن كل مجهول لا يمكن اكتسابه من أي معلوم اتفق بل لا بد له من معلومات مناسبة إياه، ولا شك أيضاً في أنه لا يمكن تحصيله من تلك المعلومات على أي وجه كانت، بل لا بد هناك من ترتیب معين فيما بينها ومن هيئة مخصوصة عارضة لها بسبب ذلك الترتيب. قوله : (من معلومات مخصوصة كالذاتيات في الحدود واللوازم البينة الشاملة في الرسوم والحدود الوسطى في الاقترانيات وقضية الملازمة في الشرطيات . قوله : ( ومن هيئة مخصوصة لا يختلجن في وهمك أن هذا القول : يقتضي أن يكون الجنس على الفصل في المعرفات واجباً ليحصل به الهيئة المخصوصة مع أن ذلك ليس بلازم عند أهل التحقيق فإن المراد من الهيئة المخصوصة فيها في الهيئة الحاصلة من انضمام أحدهما إلى الآخر، لتحصل صورة وحدانية مطابقة للمعرف سواء قدم الجنس أو الفصل. وعن الثاني منع اختصاصها بها، فإن في الترتيب ملاحظة للمرتب على وجه مخصوص، وعن الثالث ما أشرنا إليه في توجيه قوله : لتحصيل غيره وعن الرابع وضوح القرينة على أن المراد لتحصيل الغير بطريق الاكتساب .
And know that the apparent doctrine of the school of the teachers (as-hab al-ta'alim) is that speculative reasoning (nazar) is the acquisition of unknowns from knowns. Then we say: There is no doubt that every unknown cannot be acquired from any known whatsoever, but rather it must have appropriate knowns for it. And there is no doubt also that it cannot be obtained from those knowns in any manner whatsoever, but rather there must be a specific arrangement among them and a specific form (hay'a) accidental to them due to that arrangement. His saying: (From specific knowns such as essential predications (dhatiyyat) in definitions (hudud) and evident essential concomitants (lawazim bayyina shâmila) in descriptions (rusum) and middle terms (hudud wusta) in conjunctive syllogisms (iqtiranat) and the proposition of implication (qadiyat al-mulazama) in conditional syllogisms (shartiyyat). His saying: (And from a specific form) do not let it occur to your mind that this statement necessitates that the genus must precede the differentia in definientia (mu'arrafat) to obtain the specific form, whereas that is not necessary according to the verifiers (ahl al-tahqiq). For what is meant by the specific form therein is the form resulting from the conjunction of one to the other, so that a unitary form matching the definiendum (mu'arraf) is obtained, whether the genus or the differentia is presented first. And as for the second (objection), it is refuted by denying its exclusivity to them, for in the arrangement there is a consideration of the arranged in a specific manner. And as for the third, what we alluded to in explaining his saying: 'to obtain other than it'. And as for the fourth, the clarity of the evidence that what is meant is to obtain the other by way of acquisition.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of nazar (speculative reasoning) according to the school of the teachers (as-hab al-ta'alim) as 'acquisition of unknowns from knowns.' The author then carefully qualifies this definition. First, he argues that not just any known can lead to any unknown; the knowns must be appropriate (munasiba). Second, even with appropriate knowns, a specific arrangement (tartib mu'ayyan) and a resulting specific form (hay'a makhsusa) are necessary. The commentary gives examples of such specific knowns: essential predications (dhatiyyat) in definitions (hudud), evident necessary concomitants (lawazim bayyina) in descriptions (rusum), middle terms (hudud wusta) in categorical syllogisms (iqtiranat), and implication propositions (qadiyat al-mulazama) in conditional syllogisms (shartiyyat). It then addresses a potential misunderstanding: that the genus must always precede the differentia in definitions to produce the specific form. The verifiers (ahl al-tahqiq) hold that the specific form is simply the union of genus and differentia, regardless of order, as long as a unitary form matching the definiendum results. Finally, it responds to objections: the arrangement is not exclusive to one type of argument; the phrase 'to obtain other' clearly refers to acquisition, not something else.

هيئة
ذاتيات
حدود
رسوم
اقترانيات
شرطيات
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any knowns can lead to any unknown, or that the order of genus and differentia in definitions is fixed.
Correction: The author emphasizes that appropriate knowns and a specific arrangement are necessary. In definitions, the specific form results from the union of genus and differentia regardless of order.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required besides appropriate knowns for the acquisition of an unknown?
A) A greater number of knowns
B) A specific arrangement and specific form
C) Intuition without method
D) Authority of a teacher

Key Takeaway
Speculative reasoning (nazar) is a methodical process requiring appropriate knowns, precise arrangement, and a resulting form to acquire new knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek knowledge about Allah, do you ensure you are using the appropriate sources and correct reasoning, or do you assume any information will do? How might this affect the certainty of your beliefs?
?
This passage emphasizes the necessity of specific arrangement in reasoning. In your personal study of Islamic theology, have you been careless about methodology, hoping to arrive at truth haphazardly? What practical steps will you take to implement correct reasoning?
?
Are you aware that understanding Allah's attributes depends on following the proper logical procedure? If you bypass the necessary conditions, you might acquire flawed conclusions. Will you commit to studying nazar seriously before engaging in deep theological reflections?

The Two Movements of Nazar
The author describes the two movements and defines nazar as their sum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two mental movements involved in speculative reasoning (nazar) and how nazar is defined as their sum.

Core Idea
Nazar is not a single step but a process of two movements from incomplete to complete knowledge.

In this passage, the author explains how the mind moves from an initial incomplete awareness of a sought-after concept or judgment to a more complete understanding. He identifies two distinct movements: first, the mind searches through stored information to find relevant principles; second, it arranges those principles to reach the desired conclusion. The essence of nazar is the combination of these two movements.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the author's precise description of the two movements.
2
Identify the two movements
Find the description of the first movement (from incomplete awareness to principles) and the second movement (from principles to complete awareness). Note their beginnings and ends.
Look for the phrases 'مبدأ الأولى' and 'مبدأ الثانية'.
3
Understand the definition of nazar
The author defines nazar as 'the sum of these two movements'. Reflect on why both movements are necessary for the process of reasoning.
The definition is: 'فحقيقة النظر المتوسط بين المعلوم والمجهول هي مجموع هاتين الحركتين'.
4
Consider the role of arrangement
Notice that the author mentions arrangement (tartib) is necessary for the second movement but not part of the essence of nazar. This will be expanded in the next lesson.
The arrangement is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the second movement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا حصل لنا شعور ما بأمر تصوري أو تصديقي وحاولنا تحصيله على وجه أكمل، فلا بد أن يتحرك الذهن في المعلومات المخزونة عنده منتقلا من معلوم إلى آخر حتى يجد المعلومات المناسبة لذلك المطلوب وهي المسماة بمباديه، ثم لا بد أيضاً أن يتحرك تلك المبادي ليرتبها ترتيباً خاصاً يؤدي إلى ذلك المطلوب، فهناك حركتان مبدأ الأولى منهما هو المطلوب المشعور به بذلك الوجه الناقص، ومنتهاها آخر ما يحصل تلك المبادي ومبدأ الثانية أول ما يوضع منها للترتيب، ومنتهاها المطلوب المشعور به على الوجه الأكمل، فحقيقة النظر المتوسط بين المعلوم والمجهول هي مجموع هاتين الحركتين اللتين هما من قبيل الحركة في الكيفيات النفسانية، وأما الترتيب الذي ذكروه في تعريفه فهو لازم للحركة الثانية، وقلما توجد هذه الحركة بدون. قوله: (من قبيل الحركة في الكيفيات النفسانية بناء على اتحاد العلم والمعلوم فملاحظة المعلومات ليس إلا توارد الصور والكيفيات على النفس ولما كان فيها الانتقال من معلوم إلى معلوم وصورة إلى صورة دفعة ولم يكن بين المبدأ والمنتهى أمر واحد متصل قابل للانقسام إلى أمور كل واحد منها كيفية نفسانية كما في الحركة الإينية، وهو لازم في الحركة عند الحكماء وإلا لزم الجزء على ما بين في محله زاد لفظ قبيل ولم يقل وهما من الحركات النفسانية. قوله : ( وقلما توجد إلخ) إذ سنوح المبادي المناسبة دفعة عند التوجه إلى تحصيل قوله : ( ومنتهاها المطلوب المشعور به على الوجه (الأكمل فيه بحث وهو أن تحقيقه هاهنا يدل على أن كل مطلوب له وجهان فثبت ثلاثة أشياء وقد نفاه في المقصد الرابع من أن المرصد الثالث في أقسام العلم، ويمكن أن يجاب بأن منتهى الحركة الثانية نفس وجه المجهول الذي يشعر به بنفسه بالتعريف ولذا قال على الوجه الأكمل ولم يقل بالوجه الأكمل، فلا تثليث حقيقة، وإن كان ظاهر كلامه يشعر به واعلم أن اعتبار مبدأ الحركة الأولى المطلوب المشعور به بوجه ناقص ومنتهى الحركة الثانية المطلوب المشعور به على الوجه الأكمل يؤيده ما نقلته من شرح المقاصد في اثناء تقرير الوجه الثاني من متمسكي الإمام في امتناع كسبية التصور، وقد عرفت ما فيه فالظاهر أن سوق كلامه على الغالب فتدبر . قوله : ( من قبيل الحركة في الكيفيات النفسانية قيل : عليه الحركة الفكرية إنما هي في المعقولات وليست بكيفيات، وإنما الكيفيات صورها العقلية وأجيب بأن المراد الحركة في تعقلات المعقولات وهي الصور الإدراكية التي هي من باب الكيف وقد يقال : إطلاق الكيف على المعلومات على سبيل المجاز من قبيل تسمية المتبوع باسم التابع لاتحاد بينهما بحسب الذات، كما يطلقون الصور عليها ومثله كثير لا يستنكر، واعلم أن في كون هذه الحركة من قبيل الحركة في الكيفيات إشكالا نذكره إن شاء الله تعالى في مباحث الأبن على رأي الفلاسفة فليطلب هنالك . قوله : (لازم) للحركة الثانية اللزوم بحسب الوجود لكنه لازم غير محمول، فمن عرف الفكر به يقول بأنه نفس الترتيب لا باعتبار أنه لازمه .
So if we have some awareness (shu'ur) of a conceptual (tasawwuri) or assertoric (tasdiqi) matter and we attempt to obtain it in a more complete manner, then it is necessary that the mind moves in the stored information, transitioning from one known to another until it finds the appropriate information for that sought, which are called its principles (mabadi). Then it is also necessary that those principles move to arrange them in a specific order that leads to that sought. So there are two movements (harakat): the beginning of the first of them is the sought known in that incomplete aspect, and its end is the last of what obtains those principles; and the beginning of the second is the first of what is set from them for ordering, and its end is the sought known in the more complete aspect. So the reality of speculative reasoning (nazar) mediating between the known and the unknown is the sum of these two movements, which are of the category of motion in psychic qualities (kayfiyat nafsaniyya). As for the arrangement (tartib) which they mentioned in its definition, it is necessary (lazim) for the second movement, and rarely is this movement found without it. His saying: (Of the category of motion in psychic qualities) based on the unity of knowledge and the known, so observing information is nothing other than the succession of forms and qualities upon the soul, and since in it there is transition from known to known and from form to form instantaneously (daf'a), and there is not between the beginning and the end a single continuous thing divisible into things each of which is a psychic quality, as in motion in position (haraka ayniyya), and it is necessary in motion according to the philosophers, otherwise indivisible parts (juz') would be required, as established in its place. He added the word 'category' (qabil) and did not say 'they are among the psychic motions'. His saying: (And rarely is it found etc.) because the appropriate principles occur instantaneously when directing to obtain. His saying: (And its end is the sought known in the more complete aspect) contains a discussion: its verification here indicates that every sought has two aspects, thus three things are established, but he negated this in the fourth objective of the third observatory on the divisions of knowledge. It can be answered that the end of the second movement is the very aspect of the unknown which is known by itself by definition, therefore he said 'in the more complete aspect' and did not say 'by the more complete aspect', so there is no real trinity, although his wording suggests it. And know that considering the beginning of the first movement as the sought known in an incomplete aspect and the end of the second movement as the sought known in the more complete aspect is supported by what I transmitted from Sharh al-Maqasid during the exposition of the second argument of the Imams on the impossibility of the acquisition of conceptualization. You have known what is in it, but apparently his speech is on the whole, so reflect. His saying: (Of the category of motion in psychic qualities) It is said against it: intellectual motion is only in intelligibles (al-ma'qulat) and is not qualities; qualities are only their mental forms. And it is answered: what is meant is motion in the acts of understanding (ta'aqqulat) of intelligibles, which are perceptive forms that fall under the category of quality. And it may be said that applying 'quality' to information is metaphorical, like naming the followed by the name of the follower due to essential unity, just as they apply 'forms' to them, and many similar examples are not objectionable. And know that there is an issue in this motion being from the category of motion in qualities, which we will mention, God willing, in the discussions on being according to the opinion of the philosophers, so let it be sought there. His saying: (Necessary) for the second movement: the necessity is existential, but it is a non-predicative necessary. So whoever defines thought (fikr) by it says it is the very arrangement, not considering it as its necessary.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed description of the process of nazar (speculative reasoning) in terms of two mental movements. It begins by positing a situation where we have an incomplete awareness (shu'ur naqis) of a conceptual or assertoric matter and we seek to obtain it more completely. The mind first moves (taharruk) through stored information, transitioning from one known to another, until it finds the appropriate principles (mabadi). This is the first movement: from the incomplete awareness of the sought to the discovery of the principles. Then the principles themselves must move (or be moved) to arrange them in a specific order that leads to the sought. This is the second movement: from the principles arranged to the complete awareness of the sought. The reality of nazar, which mediates between the known and the unknown, is the sum (majmu') of these two movements. These movements are classified as 'from the category of motion in psychic qualities' (min qabil al-haraka fi al-kayfiyat al-nafsaniyya), not as standard physical motion. The arrangement (tartib) that is often mentioned in definitions of nazar is not the essence but a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the second movement, and rarely is the second movement found without it. The commentary then delves into why this motion is described as 'from the category' rather than simply as psychic motion, referencing the philosophers' requirement of continuity in motion, which is lacking here because the transition is instantaneous (daf'a). It also discusses a potential objection that intellectual motion involves intelligibles (ma'qulat), not psychic qualities; the answer is that it is motion in the apprehension (ta'aqqulat) of intelligibles, which are perceptual forms falling under quality. It acknowledges that calling information 'qualities' may be metaphorical but acceptable. Finally, it clarifies that the necessity of the arrangement for the second movement is existential, not conceptual, so that one could define thought (fikr) as the arrangement itself, not as its necessary concomitant.

Key Terms
شعور
تصوري
تصديقي
مبادئ
حركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think nazar is only the second movement (the reasoning process itself) or only the first movement.
Correction: The author defines nazar as the sum of both movements: the search for principles and their arrangement.

Checkpoint
What is the reality (haqiqa) of nazar according to this passage?
A) The first movement only
B) The second movement only
C) The sum of two movements
D) The arrangement (tartib) alone

Key Takeaway
Nazar is the combination of two mental movements: from incomplete awareness to principles, then from principles to complete awareness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek to understand a deep theological truth, do you consciously collect principles (mabadi) before trying to arrange them, or do you skip to conclusions? Which movement do you tend to neglect, and what is the cost?
?
Think of a time you thought you understood something but later realized your grasp was incomplete. Looking back, can you see the two movements at work—or were they missing? What would you do differently now?
?
The passage says nazar is a sum of two movements. If you only perform the first movement (gathering information) but never the second (arranging), your knowledge remains incomplete. Where in your life have you collected facts but never truly reasoned through them into conviction? Will you commit to completing both movements starting today?

Nature of Movements and Role of Arrangement
The author explains the classification of the movements and the necessity of arrangement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the movements of nazar are classified as motion in psychic qualities and how the arrangement is a necessary but non-essential aspect of the second movement.

Core Idea
The movements of nazar are mental transitions, not physical motion, and arrangement is a necessary companion, not the essence.

After defining nazar as two movements, the author discusses their nature. He clarifies that they belong to the category of motion in psychic qualities, involving instantaneous transitions between mental forms. He also addresses the role of arrangement (tartib), showing that it is a necessary concomitant of the second movement, not the essence of nazar itself. This lesson unpacks these technical points.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on psychic motion
Read the section beginning with 'قوله: (من قبيل الحركة في الكيفيات النفسانية)' and its commentary. Understand why the author uses 'from the category' rather than saying 'they are psychic motions'.
The key point is the instantaneous (daf'a) transition vs. continuous motion.
2
Examine the discussion on arrangement
Read where the author states that arrangement (tartib) is necessary (lazim) for the second movement. Note the existential necessity and the alternative definition of thought as the arrangement itself.
Look for 'قوله: (لازم)' and the following explanation.
3
Understand the objection and answer
The passage includes an objection that intellectual motion is in intelligibles, not qualities. Read the answer that it is motion in the acts of understanding (ta'aqqulat), which are qualities.
This relates to the classification as psychic qualities.
4
Reflect on the author's conclusions
Consider why the author concludes that arrangement is a necessary concomitant and what this implies for defining nazar or thought (fikr).
The author notes that some define thought as the arrangement itself, not its necessary.

فإذا حصل لنا شعور ما بأمر تصوري أو تصديقي وحاولنا تحصيله على وجه أكمل، فلا بد أن يتحرك الذهن في المعلومات المخزونة عنده منتقلا من معلوم إلى آخر حتى يجد المعلومات المناسبة لذلك المطلوب وهي المسماة ومنتهاها المطلوب المشعور به على الوجه (الأكمل فيه بحث وهو أن تحقيقه هاهنا يدل على أن كل مطلوب له وجهان فثبت ثلاثة أشياء وقد نفاه في المقصد الرابع من أن المرصد الثالث في أقسام العلم، ويمكن أن يجاب بأن في تعقلات المعقولات وهي الصور الإدراكية التي هي من باب الكيف وقد يقال : إطلاق الكيف على المعلومات على سبيل المجاز من قبيل تسمية المتبوع باسم التابع لاتحاد بينهما بحسب الذات، كما يطلقون الصور عليها ومثله كثير لا يستنكر، واعلم أن في كون هذه الحركة من قبيل الحركة في الكيفيات إشكالا نذكره إن شاء الله تعالى في مباحث الأبن على رأي الفلاسفة فليطلب هنالك . قوله : (لازم) للحركة الثانية اللزوم بحسب الوجود لكنه لازم غير محمول، فمن عرف الفكر به يقول بأنه نفس الترتيب لا باعتبار أنه لازمه .
So if we have some awareness (shu'ur) of a conceptual (tasawwuri) or assertoric (tasdiqi) matter and we attempt to obtain it in a more complete manner, then it is necessary that the mind moves in the stored information, transitioning from one known to another until it finds the appropriate information for that sought, which are called its principles (mabadi). Then it is also necessary that those principles move to arrange them in a specific order that leads to that sought. So there are two movements (harakat): the beginning of the first of them is the sought known in that incomplete aspect, and its end is the last of what obtains those principles; and the beginning of the second is the first of what is set from them for ordering, and its end is the sought known in the more complete aspect. So the reality of speculative reasoning (nazar) mediating between the known and the unknown is the sum of these two movements, which are of the category of motion in psychic qualities (kayfiyat nafsaniyya). As for the arrangement (tartib) which they mentioned in its definition, it is necessary (lazim) for the second movement, and rarely is this movement found without it. His saying: (Of the category of motion in psychic qualities) based on the unity of knowledge and the known, so observing information is nothing other than the succession of forms and qualities upon the soul, and since in it there is transition from known to known and from form to form instantaneously (daf'a), and there is not between the beginning and the end a single continuous thing divisible into things each of which is a psychic quality, as in motion in position (haraka ayniyya), and it is necessary in motion according to the philosophers, otherwise indivisible parts (juz') would be required, as established in its place. He added the word 'category' (qabil) and did not say 'they are among the psychic motions'. His saying: (And rarely is it found etc.) because the appropriate principles occur instantaneously when directing to obtain. His saying: (And its end is the sought known in the more complete aspect) contains a discussion: its verification here indicates that every sought has two aspects, thus three things are established, but he negated this in the fourth objective of the third observatory on the divisions of knowledge. It can be answered that the end of the second movement is the very aspect of the unknown which is known by itself by definition, therefore he said 'in the more complete aspect' and did not say 'by the more complete aspect', so there is no real trinity, although his wording suggests it. And know that considering the beginning of the first movement as the sought known in an incomplete aspect and the end of the second movement as the sought known in the more complete aspect is supported by what I transmitted from Sharh al-Maqasid during the exposition of the second argument of the Imams on the impossibility of the acquisition of conceptualization. You have known what is in it, but apparently his speech is on the whole, so reflect. His saying: (Of the category of motion in psychic qualities) It is said against it: intellectual motion is only in intelligibles (al-ma'qulat) and is not qualities; qualities are only their mental forms. And it is answered: what is meant is motion in the acts of understanding (ta'aqqulat) of intelligibles, which are perceptive forms that fall under the category of quality. And it may be said that applying 'quality' to information is metaphorical, like naming the followed by the name of the follower due to essential unity, just as they apply 'forms' to them, and many similar examples are not objectionable. And know that there is an issue in this motion being from the category of motion in qualities, which we will mention, God willing, in the discussions on being according to the opinion of the philosophers, so let it be sought there. His saying: (Necessary) for the second movement: the necessity is existential, but it is a non-predicative necessary. So whoever defines thought (fikr) by it says it is the very arrangement, not considering it as its necessary.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed description of the process of nazar (speculative reasoning) in terms of two mental movements. It begins by positing a situation where we have an incomplete awareness (shu'ur naqis) of a conceptual or assertoric matter and we seek to obtain it more completely. The mind first moves (taharruk) through stored information, transitioning from one known to another, until it finds the appropriate principles (mabadi). This is the first movement: from the incomplete awareness of the sought to the discovery of the principles. Then the principles themselves must move (or be moved) to arrange them in a specific order that leads to the sought. This is the second movement: from the principles arranged to the complete awareness of the sought. The reality of nazar, which mediates between the known and the unknown, is the sum (majmu') of these two movements. These movements are classified as 'from the category of motion in psychic qualities' (min qabil al-haraka fi al-kayfiyat al-nafsaniyya), not as standard physical motion. The arrangement (tartib) that is often mentioned in definitions of nazar is not the essence but a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the second movement, and rarely is the second movement found without it. The commentary then delves into why this motion is described as 'from the category' rather than simply as psychic motion, referencing the philosophers' requirement of continuity in motion, which is lacking here because the transition is instantaneous (daf'a). It also discusses a potential objection that intellectual motion involves intelligibles (ma'qulat), not psychic qualities; the answer is that it is motion in the apprehension (ta'aqqulat) of intelligibles, which are perceptual forms falling under quality. It acknowledges that calling information 'qualities' may be metaphorical but acceptable. Finally, it clarifies that the necessity of the arrangement for the second movement is existential, not conceptual, so that one could define thought (fikr) as the arrangement itself, not as its necessary concomitant.

ترتیب
لازم
فكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the arrangement (tartib) is the essence of nazar or that the movements are actual physical motions.
Correction: The arrangement is only a necessary concomitant of the second movement, and the movements are 'like' psychic motions, not actual continuous motions.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the movements are 'from the category of motion in psychic qualities' rather than 'psychic motions'?
A) Because they are not motions at all
B) Because they lack continuity and are instantaneous
C) Because they involve intelligibles not qualities
D) Because they are in the category of substance

Key Takeaway
The movements of nazar are analogous to motion in psychic qualities but are instantaneous, and arrangement is a necessary but non-essential aspect of the second movement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in deep thinking (nazar) about your beliefs, do you recognize that your mind's transitions from one idea to the next are instantaneous? What does this teach you about the need for careful, orderly reasoning in your faith? Reflect on a time your reasoning was haphazard—how can you commit to a more structured approach starting today?
?
The author distinguishes between arrangement as essential to thought (fikr) but not to nazar. In your own pursuit of knowledge, do you sometimes prioritize the external form (like eloquent arguments) over the inner substance of understanding? Can you name one area where you have done this and what you will do to seek deeper comprehension?
?
The first movement begins with an incomplete aspect of the sought known, and the second ends with a more complete aspect. Think of one Islamic belief you hold with only a vague understanding. What specific step can you take this week to move from an incomplete aspect to a more complete one through disciplined nazar?

Definition of Nazar as Two Movements in Psychic Qualities
Defines nazar as the sum of two movements and discusses tartib as a necessary consequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of nazar (theoretical reasoning) as the sum of two integrated psychic movements and the role of arrangement (tartib) as its necessary consequence.

Core Idea
Nazar is not just arrangement; it is two movements from known to unknown.

This lesson unpacks the foundational definition of nazar from the commentary on al-Mawaqif. The author explains that theoretical reasoning involves two distinct but connected mental movements, not merely the arrangement of premises. Understanding this structure is essential for grasping how kalam arguments proceed from what is known to what is sought.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition passage
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then the translation. Notice that the definition is built around two movements and a necessary consequence.
The passage is dense; focus on the first few sentences.
2
Identify the two movements
Find the description of the first movement (from deficient awareness of the sought to acquiring principles) and the second movement (from principles to complete awareness).
Each movement has a starting point and an endpoint.
3
Understand the role of tartib
The passage states that arrangement (tartib) is necessary (lazim) for the second movement, but it is not the essence of nazar. Reflect on why this distinction matters.
Some define nazar as arrangement itself, but the author clarifies it is a consequence.
4
Consider the category of motion
Note that these movements are described as being 'of the category of motion in psychic qualities.' Think about what this means for the nature of thought.
This relates to the unity of knowledge and the known.

The fifth observatory—the first objective: on its definition. The appropriate items of information for that sought thing, which are called its principles, then it is also necessary that those principles be moved to arrange them in a special order leading to that sought thing. Thus there are two movements: the starting point of the first of them is the sought thing known in that deficient mode, and its endpoint is the last of what is obtained of those principles; and the starting point of the second is the first of what is placed from them for arrangement, and its endpoint is the sought thing known in the most complete mode. So the reality of theoretical reasoning (al-naẓar) intermediate between the known and the unknown is the sum of these two movements, which are of the category of motion in psychic qualities (al-kayfiyyāt al-nafsāniyya). As for the arrangement (al-tartīb) which they mentioned in its definition, it is necessary for the second movement, and rarely is this movement found without [it]. From his statement: “(Of the category of motion in psychic qualities) based on the unity of knowledge and the known, so observation of items of knowledge is nothing but the successive occurrence of forms and qualities upon the soul; and since there is in it transition from known to known and from form to form at once, and there is not between the start and the end a single continuous thing divisible into things each of which is a psychic quality, as in locative motion, and it is necessary in motion according to the philosophers—otherwise the part would be necessary, as explained in its place—he added the word ‘category’ and did not say ‘and they are of psychic movements.’ His statement: “(And rarely is this movement found etc.)—because the occurrence of the appropriate principles at once when directing towards acquisition. His statement: “(And its endpoint is the sought thing known in the most complete mode) contains an investigation: his verification here indicates that every sought thing has two modes, so three things are established, and he negated that in the fourth objective of the third observatory on the divisions of knowledge. It can be answered that the endpoint of the second movement is the very mode of the unknown which is perceived by definition, so he said ‘in the most complete mode’ and did not say ‘by the most complete mode,’ so there is no real trinity, although the apparent meaning of his words suggests it. Know that the consideration of the start of the first movement as the sought thing known in a deficient mode and the endpoint of the second movement as the sought thing known in the most complete mode is supported by what I transmitted from Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid during the exposition of the second aspect of the supporters of the Imām [al-Rāzī] regarding the impossibility of acquisition (kasb) of concepts—and you have known what is in it, so apparently his flow of speech is predominant—so ponder. His statement: “(Of the category of motion in psychic qualities): It was objected that intellectual motion is in intelligibles, not in qualities; qualities are only their mental forms. It was answered that what is meant is motion in the intellections of intelligibles, which are the perceptual forms that belong to the category of quality. It may be said that applying ‘quality’ to items of knowledge is metaphorical, of the type of naming the followed by the name of the follower due to their essential unity, as they apply ‘forms’ to them—and such is common and not objectionable. Know that there is an objection to this motion being of the category of motion in qualities, which we will mention, God willing, in the discussions on being according to the philosophers—so let it be sought there. His statement: “(Necessary) for the second movement: necessity according to existence, but it is a necessary consequence that is not predicated. So whoever defines theoretical reasoning (al-fikr) by it says it is the very arrangement, not insofar as it is its necessary consequence.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the term 'nazar' (theoretical reasoning) from the commentary on al-Mawaqif. It begins by stating the objective: the definition of nazar. The author explains that appropriate pieces of information (the principles) are necessary, and they must be moved and arranged in a special order to reach the sought knowledge. He then distinguishes two movements (حركتان): the first movement starts from the deficient awareness of the sought thing and ends with obtaining the principles; the second movement starts from the arrangement of those principles and ends with the complete awareness of the sought thing. The reality of nazar is the sum of these two movements. He clarifies that these movements are of the category of motion in psychic qualities. The arrangement (tartib) mentioned in other definitions is a necessary consequence (لازم) of the second movement, and rarely occurs without it. The rest of the passage addresses glosses on specific phrases: the justification for calling it 'of the category' rather than simply 'psychic movements'; the objection about motion in intelligibles vs. qualities; the resolution of an apparent trinity in the description of the sought thing; and the final gloss that the arrangement is a necessary consequence that is not predicated (i.e., it is a 'لازم غير محمول'), so some define nazar as the arrangement itself rather than its necessary consequence.

اللازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nazar is simply the act of arranging premises (tartib) to deduce a conclusion.
Correction: According to this passage, nazar is the sum of two movements: the first to acquire principles and the second to arrange them. Tartib is only a necessary consequence of the second movement, not the essence of nazar.

Checkpoint
According to the definition in this passage, what is the reality (haqiqa) of nazar (theoretical reasoning)?
A) The arrangement of known items to reach the unknown.
B) The sum of two movements: from the deficient sought to the principles, and from the principles to the complete sought.
C) The first movement only, from the sought to the principles.
D) The second movement only, involving the arrangement.

Key Takeaway
Nazar is the sum of two psychic movements: from deficient awareness of the sought to the principles, and from arranging the principles to complete awareness; arrangement is a necessary consequence but not the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you set out to understand a religious issue, do you first recognize your own deficient awareness, or do you skip straight to arranging arguments? What would change if you consciously made the first movement?
?
The movements of nazar are 'psychic qualities' — inner states. How often do you pay attention to your inner psychological state when reasoning about faith? Are you aware of the journey from confusion to certainty within your soul?
?
If nazar is not just arrangement but two movements, are you willing to slow down and actually move from deficient to complete awareness today? What is one belief you have that you have not fully reasoned through this way?

Definition of Nazar and Its Division into Valid and Invalid
Definition of nazar and its division into valid and invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of nazar as an arrangement of judgments leading to a desired conclusion, and how nazar is divided into valid and invalid based on whether it leads to the desired objective.

Core Idea
Nazar is valid when it leads to the desired objective; otherwise it is invalid.

This passage establishes the foundational definition of nazar (rational consideration) and its essential division. The commentator clarifies that validity and invalidity are real attributes of nazar, not mere metaphors. Understanding this framework is crucial for evaluating theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of nazar
Carefully read the Arabic and translation of the opening section, noting the definition given by Imam al-Razi and the commentator's explanation.
The definition involves arrangement (tartib) of judgments (tasdiqat).
2
Identify the division into valid and invalid
Find where the passage divides nazar into ṣaḥīḥ (valid) and fāsid (invalid) and note the criterion for each.
3
Understand the reality of validity/invalidity
The commentator stresses that these are real attributes, not metaphorical. Reflect on why this matters for theological method.
Contrast with the view in Sharh al-Maqasid mentioned later.
4
Examine the definition by necessary concomitant
The commentator explains that the definition of nazar by arrangement is actually a definition by necessary concomitant. Understand the reasoning behind this.
This is a key logical nuance in kalam.

المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثاني : أنه يؤدي إلى المطلوب وفاسد يقابله الأولى، بل الأكثر أن ينتقل أولاً من المطالب إلى المبادي ثم منها إلى المطالب، ولا الغفلات، خفاء في أن هذا الترتيب يستلزم التوجه إلى المطلوب، وتجريد الذهن عن وتحديق العقل نحو المعقولات فتأمل، واعلم أيضاً أن الإمام الرازي عرف النظر بترتيب تصديقات يتوصل بها إلى تصديقات أخر، بناء على ما اختاره من امتناع الكسب في التصورات . المقصد الثاني : أنه يؤدي إلى المطلوب وفاسد يقابله ( أنه ( أي النظر ينقسم إلى صحيح وهو الذي يؤدي إلى المطلوب وفاسد يقابله ( أي لا يؤدي إلى المطلوب فالصحة والفساد صفتان عارضتان للنظر حقيقة لا مجازاً، لكنه أراد أن يبين السبب في اتصافه بهما فقال: (ولما كان المختار) عند المتأخرين مذهب أهل التعليم وهو أنه ترتيب العلوم بحيث يؤدي إلى هيئة مخصوصة . مطلوب نظري قليل وإذا كان كذلك، فالترتيب يكون لازماً للحركتين في التحقيق، فتعريف النظر به تعریف باللازم، فإن جوزناً التعريف باللازم الغير المحمول فذاك، وإلا حملنا الكلام على التسامح بأن المراد بالترتيب ما به الترتيب كما في تعريف الحكمة ساستمكمال النفس، أو على الاصطلاح على ذلك . قوله : ( وتحديق العقل إلخ) حمل الشارح المعقولات على المبادي التي تقع الحركة فيها على خلاف ما نقله سابقاً، وهو الحق إذ الوجدان شاهد صدق على أنه لا يلزم لنا بعد التوجه إلى المطلوب، استحضار المبادي وتحديق النظر في مناسبتها وترتيبها فتأمل قوله : حتى يظهر لك أن هذه التعريفات كلها تعريفات باللوازم وحقيقة النظر، وحقيقة النظر هي الحركتان وأن لا نزاع في الحقيقة بين الفريقين . قوله : ( وهو الذي يؤدي إلخ) بيان للحاصل وإشارة إلى أن قوله يؤدي صفة كاشفة لا أن في العبارة تقدير المبتدأ والموصول . قوله : ( وتحديق العقل نحو المعقولات فتأمل مراد الشارح بالمعقولات هو المبادي، وأما مراد المصنف بها فهو المطالب لأن الكلام هناك مسوق على انتفاء الاستعانة بالمعلومات السابقة بخلافه ههنا، فالمتوجه إليه والمحدق نحوه متغايران فيما ذكر الشارح هاهنا، بخلافهما فيما نقله عن الأبهري وقد يقال : التمهيد السابق يدل على أن التحديق أيضا نحو المطلوب، وهذا يشعر بأنه نحو المبادي، وهذا هو الظاهر لكن الفرق بين التوجه إلى المطلوب وتحديق العقل نحوه، لا يخلو عن خفاء، اللهم إلا أن يحمل أحدهما على التوجه في الجملة، والآخر على التوجه التام هذا وكأن الأمر بالتأمل إشارة إلى ما دل عليه كلام المصنف من أن التفسير بالتحديق عن الغفلات لمن لا يرى النظر لاكتساب المجهولات من المعلومات ليس بمقطوع به لجواز أن يكون تعريفا باللازم، لكن الكلام في محمولية هذه اللوازم حتى يصح التعريف بها عند من لا يجوز التعريف بالمباين. قوله : (إلى) صحيح يؤدي إلى المطلوب أي يؤدي نوعه فلا يرد على تعريف الصحيح والفاسد بانتفاء الطرد والعكس، قولنا زيد حمار وكل حمار جسم، ومجرد حمل الأداء على [؟] . قوله : ( يؤدي إلى المطلوب إلخ) قيل يرد على التعريفين قولنا زيد حمار وكل حمار جسم، فإنه يدخل في الصحيح مع أنه فاسد المادة، أقول : لا نسلم تأديته إلى المطلوب، فإن حقيقة القياس على ماصرح به الشارح في حواشيه على شرح المختصر وسط مستلزم للأكبر ثابت للأصغر وهاهنا لا يثبت الأوسط للأصغر فلا اندراج فلا تأدية في نفس الأمر، نعم إنه يؤدي بعد تسليم المقدمتين . قوله : ( فالصحة إلخ) رد لما في شرح المقاصد من أن صحة النظر، وفساده عبارة عن صحة مادته وصورته ففي انقسامه إلى الصحيح والفاسد تجوز كما في انقسامه إلى الجلي، والخفي .
The fifth observation – the second aim: that it leads to the desired and its invalid opposite. The first, nay the more frequent [approach] is that one first moves from the objectives to the principles and then from them to the objectives, and not from heedlessness, hidden [that] this arrangement necessitates turning towards the desired, and emptying the mind [of distractions] and focusing the intellect towards the intelligibles, so reflect. And know also that Imam al-Razi defined nazar as an arrangement of judgments by which other judgments are reached, based on his choice of the impossibility of acquisition in concepts. The second aim: that it leads to the desired and its invalid opposite. (That is, nazar divides into valid – which leads to the desired – and invalid – which does not lead to the desired. So validity and invalidity are two attributes that occur to nazar in reality, not metaphorically. But he wanted to show the reason for its being described by them, so he said: 'And since the chosen [view] among the later scholars is the school of ta'lim, which is that it is an arrangement of sciences such that it leads to a specific form.' A theoretical objective is little; and if that is the case, then arrangement is necessary for the two movements in verification. Thus defining nazar by it is a definition by necessary concomitant. If we allow definition by the non-predicated necessary concomitant, then that [is acceptable]; otherwise we carry the speech as a tolerance, meaning by arrangement that which has arrangement, as in the definition of wisdom as 'perfection of the soul' or as a convention. His statement: 'and focusing the intellect towards the intelligibles, etc.' The commentator interprets 'the intelligibles' as the principles in which movement occurs, contrary to what he transmitted earlier, and that is correct, for inner experience bears witness that after turning towards the desired object, it is not necessary for us to bring to mind the principles and focus the intellect on their suitability and arrangement, so reflect on his statement until it becomes clear to you that all these definitions are definitions by concomitants and the reality of nazar, and the reality of nazar is the two movements, and there is no dispute in reality between the two groups. His statement: 'and it is that which leads, etc.' is an explanation of the result and an indication that his statement 'leads' is a descriptive attribute, not that there is in the expression an implied subject and relative pronoun. His statement: 'and focusing the intellect towards the intelligibles, so reflect' – the commentator means by 'the intelligibles' the principles, while the author's meaning by them is the objectives, because the speech there is based on the negation of using previous information, contrary to here. So the object turned towards and the object focused towards are different in what the commentator mentions here, unlike what he transmitted from al-Abhari. It may be said: the previous preamble indicates that the focusing is also towards the desired object, and this suggests that it is towards the principles, and this is apparent, but the difference between turning towards the desired and focusing the intellect towards it is not free from obscurity, unless one of them is interpreted as general attention and the other as complete attention. And it is as if the command to reflect is an indication of what the author's speech indicates, that the interpretation of 'focusing away from heedlessness' for one who does not consider nazar as acquisition of unknowns from knowns is not certain, because it is possible that it is a definition by concomitant, but the discussion is about the predicability of these concomitants so that definition by them is valid for one who does not allow definition by the separate. His statement: 'to' – valid leads to the desired, meaning its type leads, so it is not affected by the definition of valid and invalid by lack of comprehensiveness and exclusiveness, our statement 'Zayd is a donkey and every donkey is a body', and merely applying the leading to [?]. His statement: 'leads to the desired, etc.' It is said that an objection applies to the two definitions: our statement 'Zayd is a donkey and every donkey is a body', for it enters into the valid despite being invalid in matter. I say: we do not concede that it leads to the desired, for the reality of the syllogism according to what the commentator clarified in his glosses on the commentary of al-Mukhtasar al-wasat requires the major [term] to be established for the minor, and here the middle term is not established for the minor, so there is no inclusion, so there is no leading in actual fact. Yes, it leads after the two premises are granted. His statement: 'and validity, etc.' is a refutation of what is in Sharh al-Maqasid that validity of nazar and its invalidity are expressions of the validity of its matter and form, so in its division into valid and invalid there is metaphor, as in its division into evident and hidden.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of nazar (rational consideration) and its division into valid and invalid. The commentator begins by noting that the more frequent method is to move from objectives to principles, and then from principles to objectives, and that this arrangement requires turning towards the desired and focusing the intellect towards the intelligibles. He then mentions that Imam al-Razi defined nazar as an arrangement of judgments that leads to other judgments, based on his denial of acquisition in concepts. The commentator then explains the second aim: that nazar leads to the desired or its opposite, so nazar is divided into valid (leading to the desired) and invalid (not leading). Validity and invalidity are real, not metaphorical attributes of nazar. The commentator then discusses the reason for nazar being described by these attributes: the chosen view among later scholars is the school of ta'lim, which considers nazar as an arrangement of sciences leading to a specific form. He then addresses the issue that the definition of nazar by arrangement is actually a definition by necessary concomitant, because arrangement is a concomitant of the two movements that constitute nazar. He considers whether definitions by non-predicated concomitants are allowed. Then he provides a detailed analysis of the phrase 'focusing the intellect towards the intelligibles', discussing different interpretations by the commentator (al-Jurjani) and the author (al-Iji). The commentator interprets 'intelligibles' as principles, while the author means objectives. This difference arises from different contexts. He also addresses the relation between 'turning towards' and 'focusing'. He concludes that the definition of nazar by requirement of focusing away from heedlessness is not certain because it may be a definition by concomitant. He then comments on the word 'to' in 'leads to the desired', explaining that it means its type leads, to avoid a potential objection about comprehensiveness. Finally, he presents an objection using the example 'Zayd is a donkey, every donkey is a body' which appears to be a valid syllogism leading to a conclusion but is actually invalid in matter. The answer is that it does not truly lead because the middle term is not established for the minor. After the premises are granted, it leads, but the syllogism is not sound. The last paragraph refutes the view in Sharh al-Maqasid that validity and invalidity of nazar are merely about its matter and form, and that the division into valid and invalid is metaphorical. The commentator insists that validity and invalidity are real attributes of nazar itself.

تعريف باللازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the definition of nazar as arrangement is a proper definition by genus and differentia.
Correction: The commentator clarifies it is a definition by necessary concomitant (تعريف باللازم), not an essential definition.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, how is nazar divided?
A) Into valid and invalid based on matter and form.
B) Into valid (leading to the desired) and invalid (not leading).
C) Into clear and hidden.
D) Into essential and accidental.

Key Takeaway
Nazar is defined as an arrangement of judgments leading to a desired conclusion, and it is divided into valid (leading to the desired) and invalid (not leading); this division is based on real attributes, not metaphor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in thinking about your faith, do you ever find that your reflections lead you to the truth (the desired objective) or do they sometimes lead you away? What does that say about the validity of your nazar?
?
The lesson distinguishes real validity from metaphorical. In your own life, are you sometimes content with a 'metaphorical' validity—thinking you have arrived at truth when you haven't truly examined the principles? Challenge yourself: How can you ensure your intellectual efforts are truly leading you to the desired knowledge of Allah?
?
Are you aware of the two movements in nazar: from objectives to principles and back? Do you consciously structure your theological reflections in this way, or do you jump to conclusions? How might adopting this method change your understanding of critical aqeedah issues?

What Is Kalam?
advanced
7 min
Focusing the Intellect and the Distinction between Principles and Objectives
Analysis of 'focusing the intellect' phrase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different interpretations of 'focusing the intellect towards the intelligibles' and the distinction between principles (mabadi) and objectives (matalib) in the process of nazar.

Core Idea
Focusing the intellect can mean either turning towards the desired object or concentrating on the principles.

The commentator delves into the phrase 'focusing the intellect towards the intelligibles' (تحديق العقل نحو المعقولات). He shows that the author and commentator differ on whether 'intelligibles' refers to principles or objectives, and that there is a subtle difference between general turning and complete focusing. This lesson clarifies these nuances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on 'focusing the intellect'
Read the Arabic and translation of the section beginning with 'قوله : ( وتحديق العقل إلخ)'.
Pay attention to the different interpretations.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Note that the commentator (al-Jurjani) interprets 'intelligibles' as principles, while the author (al-Iji) elsewhere means objectives. Understand the context for each.
The shift in meaning is due to different contexts in the text.
3
Distinguish between 'turning towards' and 'focusing'
The commentator finds a difference between general turning towards the desired and complete focusing. Reflect on why this distinction matters for the definition of nazar.
The command to reflect (فتأمل) indicates a subtle point.
4
Understand the overall purpose
This analysis shows that all definitions of nazar are by concomitants and that there is no real dispute about the essence of nazar.
The essence is the two movements.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of nazar (rational consideration) and its division into valid and invalid. The commentator begins by noting that the more frequent method is to move from objectives to principles, and then from principles to objectives, and that this arrangement requires turning towards the desired and focusing the intellect towards the intelligibles. He then addresses the issue that the definition of nazar by arrangement is actually a definition by necessary concomitant, because arrangement is a concomitant of the two movements that constitute nazar. He considers whether definitions by non-predicated concomitants are allowed. Then he provides a detailed analysis of the phrase 'focusing the intellect towards the intelligibles', discussing different interpretations by the commentator (al-Jurjani) and the author (al-Iji). The commentator interprets 'intelligibles' as principles, while the author means objectives. This difference arises from different contexts. He also addresses the relation between 'turning towards' and 'focusing'. He concludes that the definition of nazar by requirement of focusing away from heedlessness is not certain because it may be a definition by concomitant. He then comments on the word 'to' in 'leads to the desired', explaining that it means its type leads, to avoid a potential objection about comprehensiveness. Finally, he presents an objection using the example 'Zayd is a donkey, every donkey is a body' which appears to be a valid syllogism leading to a conclusion but is actually invalid in matter. The answer is that it does not truly lead because the middle term is not established for the minor. After the premises are granted, it leads, but the syllogism is not sound. The last paragraph refutes the view in Sharh al-Maqasid that validity and invalidity of nazar are merely about its matter and form, and that the division into valid and invalid is metaphorical. The commentator insists that validity and invalidity are real attributes of nazar itself.

The phrase 'focusing the intellect towards the intelligibles' is interpreted differently by the author and commentator, reflecting a nuanced distinction between general orientation towards the desired and focused concentration on the principles.

Objection to the Definition of Valid Nazar and Its Refutation
Objection and refutation using a syllogism with false premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection raised against the definition of valid nazar using the example 'Zayd is a donkey, every donkey is a body', and how the commentator refutes it by distinguishing between formal validity and material soundness.

Core Idea
A syllogism with a false premise does not truly lead to the desired conclusion, even if its form is valid.

The commentator addresses a potential counterexample: the syllogism 'Zayd is a donkey, every donkey is a body' appears to lead to a conclusion, but it is actually invalid in matter. This lesson explores why such an argument does not constitute valid nazar and how the commentator refutes the objection and also rejects the metaphorical view of validity in Sharh al-Maqasid.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the example
Read the Arabic and translation of the section beginning with 'قوله : (إلى) صحيح يؤدي إلى المطلوب'.
The example is: Zayd is a donkey, every donkey is a body.
2
Understand why it seems valid
The form is valid: major term (body) is affirmed for the middle (donkey), and the middle is affirmed for the minor (Zayd), so body would be affirmed for Zayd.
3
The commentator's refutation
The middle term (donkey) is not actually established for the minor (Zayd) because the premise 'Zayd is a donkey' is false. So the syllogism does not truly lead to the conclusion.
True leading requires sound premises.
4
Note the refutation of Sharh al-Maqasid
The commentator rejects the view that validity and invalidity are merely metaphorical attributes related to matter and form; they are real attributes of nazar itself.

The fifth observation – the second aim: that it leads to the desired and its invalid opposite. The first, nay the more frequent [approach] is that one first moves from the objectives to the principles and then from them to the objectives, and not from heedlessness, hidden [that] this arrangement necessitates turning towards the desired, and emptying the mind [of distractions] and focusing the intellect towards the intelligibles, so reflect. And know also that Imam al-Razi defined nazar as an arrangement of judgments by which other judgments are reached, based on his choice of the impossibility of acquisition in concepts. The second aim: that it leads to the desired and its invalid opposite. (That is, nazar divides into valid – which leads to the desired – and invalid – which does not lead to the desired. So validity and invalidity are two attributes that occur to nazar in reality, not metaphorically. But he wanted to show the reason for its being described by them, so he said: 'And since the chosen [view] among the later scholars is the school of ta'lim, which is that it is an arrangement of sciences such that it leads to a specific form.' A theoretical objective is little; and if that is the case, then arrangement is necessary for the two movements in verification. Thus defining nazar by it is a definition by necessary concomitant. If we allow definition by the non-predicated necessary concomitant, then that [is acceptable]; otherwise we carry the speech as a tolerance, meaning by arrangement that which has arrangement, as in the definition of wisdom as 'perfection of the soul' or as a convention. His statement: 'and focusing the intellect towards the intelligibles, etc.' The commentator interprets 'the intelligibles' as the principles in which movement occurs, contrary to what he transmitted earlier, and that is correct, for inner experience bears witness that after turning towards the desired object, it is not necessary for us to bring to mind the principles and focus the intellect on their suitability and arrangement, so reflect on his statement until it becomes clear to you that all these definitions are definitions by concomitants and the reality of nazar, and the reality of nazar is the two movements, and there is no dispute in reality between the two groups. His statement: 'and it is that which leads, etc.' is an explanation of the result and an indication that his statement 'leads' is a descriptive attribute, not that there is in the expression an implied subject and relative pronoun. His statement: 'and focusing the intellect towards the intelligibles, so reflect' – the commentator means by 'the intelligibles' the principles, while the author's meaning by them is the objectives, because the speech there is based on the negation of using previous information, contrary to here. So the object turned towards and the object focused towards are different in what the commentator mentions here, unlike what he transmitted from al-Abhari. It may be said: the previous preamble indicates that the focusing is also towards the desired object, and this suggests that it is towards the principles, and this is apparent, but the difference between turning towards the desired and focusing the intellect towards it is not free from obscurity, unless one of them is interpreted as general attention and the other as complete attention. And it is as if the command to reflect is an indication of what the author's speech indicates, that the interpretation of 'focusing away from heedlessness' for one who does not consider nazar as acquisition of unknowns from knowns is not certain, because it is possible that it is a definition by concomitant, but the discussion is about the predicability of these concomitants so that definition by them is valid for one who does not allow definition by the separate. His statement: 'to' – valid leads to the desired, meaning its type leads, so it is not affected by the definition of valid and invalid by lack of comprehensiveness and exclusiveness, our statement 'Zayd is a donkey and every donkey is a body', and merely applying the leading to [?]. His statement: 'leads to the desired, etc.' It is said that an objection applies to the two definitions: our statement 'Zayd is a donkey and every donkey is a body', for it enters into the valid despite being invalid in matter. I say: we do not concede that it leads to the desired, for the reality of the syllogism according to what the commentator clarified in his glosses on the commentary of al-Mukhtasar al-wasat requires the major [term] to be established for the minor, and here the middle term is not established for the minor, so there is no inclusion, so there is no leading in actual fact. Yes, it leads after the two premises are granted. His statement: 'and validity, etc.' is a refutation of what is in Sharh al-Maqasid that validity of nazar and its invalidity are expressions of the validity of its matter and form, so in its division into valid and invalid there is metaphor, as in its division into evident and hidden.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of nazar (rational consideration) and its division into valid and invalid. The commentator begins by noting that the more frequent method is to move from objectives to principles, and then from principles to objectives, and that this arrangement requires turning towards the desired and focusing the intellect towards the intelligibles. He then mentions that Imam al-Razi defined nazar as an arrangement of judgments that leads to other judgments, based on his denial of acquisition in concepts. The commentator then explains the second aim: that nazar leads to the desired or its opposite, so nazar is divided into valid (leading to the desired) and invalid (not leading). Validity and invalidity are real, not metaphorical attributes of nazar. The commentator then discusses the reason for nazar being described by these attributes: the chosen view among later scholars is the school of ta'lim, which considers nazar as an arrangement of sciences leading to a specific form. He then addresses the issue that the definition of nazar by arrangement is actually a definition by necessary concomitant, because arrangement is a concomitant of the two movements that constitute nazar. He considers whether definitions by non-predicated concomitants are allowed. Then he provides a detailed analysis of the phrase 'focusing the intellect towards the intelligibles', discussing different interpretations by the commentator (al-Jurjani) and the author (al-Iji). The commentator interprets 'intelligibles' as principles, while the author means objectives. This difference arises from different contexts. He also addresses the relation between 'turning towards' and 'focusing'. He concludes that the definition of nazar by requirement of focusing away from heedlessness is not certain because it may be a definition by concomitant. He then comments on the word 'to' in 'leads to the desired', explaining that it means its type leads, to avoid a potential objection about comprehensiveness. Finally, he presents an objection using the example 'Zayd is a donkey, every donkey is a body' which appears to be a valid syllogism leading to a conclusion but is actually invalid in matter. The answer is that it does not truly lead because the middle term is not established for the minor. After the premises are granted, it leads, but the syllogism is not sound. The last paragraph refutes the view in Sharh al-Maqasid that validity and invalidity of nazar are merely about its matter and form, and that the division into valid and invalid is metaphorical. The commentator insists that validity and invalidity are real attributes of nazar itself.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any valid-looking syllogism constitutes valid nazar, even if its premises are false.
Correction: The commentator shows that valid nazar requires the premises to be true for the conclusion to be truly obtained; a false premise prevents the inference from being actual.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator reject the example 'Zayd is a donkey, every donkey is a body' as valid nazar?
A) Because the form is invalid.
B) Because the middle term is not established for the minor.
C) Because the conclusion is false.
D) Because it is a definition by concomitant.

Key Takeaway
Valid nazar requires both sound form and sound matter; a syllogism with a false premise cannot truly lead to the desired conclusion, and validity/invalidity are real attributes of nazar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a convincing argument, do you ever stop to check whether its premises are actually true, or do you assume they are because the conclusion seems logical? What harmful belief might you be holding because you never questioned its foundation?
?
Think of a time you used reasoning to justify an action or belief—did you verify that the middle term (the connecting fact) truly applies to you, or did you assume it? How might your life change if you rigorously tested the 'matter' of your reasoning?
?
You believe in certain theological truths based on proofs. But have you ever accepted a proof because its form seemed sound, without ensuring its premises are absolutely certain? Commit this week to examine one of your core beliefs and ask: 'Is the middle term truly established for me?'
