Refuting the Innovation Objection: Good Innovation in Kalam
Refutation of the objection that kalam is a blameworthy innovation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator refutes the objection that kalam is a blameworthy innovation by distinguishing between absolute novelty and terminological novelty, and by classifying innovation based on underlying interests.

Core Idea
Not all innovation is blameworthy; some are good based on their benefits.

This passage directly confronts the objection that the science of kalam is a blameworthy innovation (bid'ah). The commentator presents a nuanced two-part response: either the objector claims kalam in its absolute form is an innovation, or that its later terminological development is an innovation. Each claim receives a different answer, leading to a broader classification of innovation that depends on whether it disrupts an established ruling or brings about beneficial interests.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the passage while keeping in mind that the interlocutor claims kalam is a 'bid'ah' (innovation). The commentator's response is structured around two possible interpretations of this claim.
Identify the two possible claims: absolute novelty vs. terminological novelty.
2
Analyze the two-part response
Notice that the commentator first denies the minor premise (that absolute engagement in kalam is an innovation) by citing tawatur. Then he concedes the second claim but reclassifies it as a 'good innovation' (bid'ah hasanah), drawing an analogy with fiqh.
The commentary clarifies that 'فهي' functions as a predicate indicating some innovations are good.
3
Explore the classification of innovation
The passage ends with a general rule: if an innovation abolishes a fixed ruling, it is rejected; otherwise, it is divided into obligatory, recommended, or permissible based on the interests it serves.
Examples given include codifying sciences and building schools.
4
Connect to the learning objective
Reflect on how this classification defends kalam as a beneficial science, not a blameworthy innovation.
This shows that kalam, like other developed disciplines, is a valid tool for preserving and understanding faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم إنه منع لكلية الكبرى القائلة كل بدعة، رد وتحرير الجواب أنك إن ادعيت أن النبي وأصحابه لم يشتغلوا بالأبحاث الكلامية أصلاً، فالاشتغال بها مطلقاً بدعة فهو ممنوع لما ذكرناه من التواتر الذي لا شبهة فيه، وإن ادعيت أن الاشتغال بها على هذه الاصطلاحات والتفاصيل بدعة فهو مسلم، لكنه بدعة حسنة لا مردودة كالاشتغال بالفقه وسائر العلوم الشرعية. فهي خبر المبتدأ مطلقاً، ومعطوفة على خبر محذوف عند من لم يجوز أي مجمل الكلام المذكور بقوله نعم إلخ، منع الكبرى فمن البدعة ما هي حسنة. قوله : ( هذا إشارة إلخ) كما أن ما قبله منع للصغرى أي الاشتغال بالكلام بدعة، فصار الجواب مردداً بين منع الصغرى ومنع الكبرى. قوله: (لكنه بدعة حسنة) قالوا إن البدعة إن تضمن رفع أمر ثابت في الشرع، مردودة وإلا فهي منقسمة إلى الواجب والمندوب والمباح على حسب المصالح التي تتضمنها، كتدوين العلوم الشرعية وبناء المدارس والمرابط، والتنعم في المأكل والمشارب والملابس.
Yes, it is a refutation of the universality of the major premise which says 'every innovation is blameworthy'. The response and clarification of the answer is that if you claim that the Prophet and his Companions did not engage in theological investigations at all, then engaging in them absolutely is an innovation — that is prevented by what we have mentioned of indisputable tawatur (mass transmission). And if you claim that engaging in them with these terminologies and details is an innovation, then it is conceded, but it is a good innovation (bid'ah hasanah), not rejected, like engagement in fiqh and all other religious sciences. 'She' (فهي) is the predicate of the subject absolutely, and conjoined to a deleted predicate according to those who do not allow [it], i.e., the summary of the mentioned speech by his saying 'yes' etc., a refutation of the major premise, for among innovations there is what is good. His saying: 'this indicates etc.' Just as what preceded it is a refutation of the minor premise, i.e., engagement in kalam is an innovation, so the answer becomes disjunctive between refuting the minor premise and refuting the major premise. His saying: 'but it is a good innovation' — they said: if the innovation involves abolishing an established matter in the Law, it is rejected; otherwise it is divided into obligatory, recommended, and permissible according to the interests (masalih) it contains, such as codifying religious sciences, building schools and fortresses, and enjoying good food, drink, and clothing.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a response to the first objection that kalam is a blameworthy innovation. The commentator explains that the response has two alternatives: either kalam in its absolute form (as the early scholars did not practice it) is claimed to be an innovation — this is denied because there is tawatur (mass transmission) of the Companions engaging in it in principle; or kalam with its later terminologies and details is claimed to be an innovation — this is conceded, but it is a good innovation (bid'ah hasanah), analogous to fiqh. The commentator then provides a grammatical gloss on the word 'فهي' from the original text, clarifying that it functions as a predicate indicating that some innovations are good. He then cites the classification of innovation: if it contradicts a fixed ruling, it is rejected; otherwise it may be obligatory, recommended, or permissible based on the benefits it brings, giving examples like codifying sciences, building schools, and permissible enjoyments. This reframes the discussion from a binary 'all innovation is bad' to a nuanced classification grounded in maqasid.

كبرى

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All innovation (bid'ah) in religious matters is automatically prohibited.
Correction: The passage shows that innovation is not monolithic; it is classified based on whether it contradicts a fixed ruling and the benefits it brings. Some innovations are good, such as developing new sciences or building schools.

Key Takeaway
Innovation in religious matters is not automatically blameworthy; it must be evaluated based on whether it contradicts established rulings or serves legitimate interests.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Is there a beneficial activity in your life—perhaps in seeking Islamic knowledge or serving the community—that you have hesitated to engage in because you feared it might be bid'ah? What step can you take today to act on a good innovation that serves a clear religious interest?

Distinction Between Nazar and Jadal
The commentator defines nazar and jadal and explains their logical ordering.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between nazar (reasoning) and jadal (debate) as well as the logical ordering principle used in argumentation.

Core Idea
Nazar is reflection; jadal is debate to compel—knowing the difference guards against conflating theology with mere argument.

In this brief but crucial passage, the commentator distinguishes two activities that are often confused: nazar (reasoning) and jadal (debate). He also explains why the order of presenting arguments matters, linking it to the structure of a logical syllogism. Let's unpack these definitions and see how they shape Islamic theological method.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definitions
Read the passage and note how nazar is defined as 'fikr' (thought) and jadal as 'mubahathah li-ilzam al-ghayr' (discussion to compel the other). These are different purposes.
This distinction is foundational for understanding the nature of kalam.
2
See the logical ordering principle
The commentator explains why the argument that kalam involves nazar (rather than jadal) is presented here: it refutes the minor premise of an earlier objection, while the earlier response refuted the major premise. This shows careful argumentation.
This reflects the structured nature of kalam discourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( والنظر غير الجدل فإن الجدل هو المباحثة لإلزام الغير والنظر هو الفكر، ولا يخفى أن قانون التوجيه يقتضي تقديم هذا لأنه منع الصغرى وما تقدم منع الكبرى.
His saying: 'And nazar (reasoning) is different from jadal (debate). For jadal is discussion to compel the other, and nazar is thought. And it is not hidden that the rule of presentation requires presenting this (argument) because it is a refutation of the minor premise, while what preceded is a refutation of the major premise.'
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This short passage defines two key terms: nazar (reasoning) and jadal (debate). Nazar is intellectual reflection (fikr), while jadal is a dialogical process aimed at compelling an opponent (ilzam al-ghayr). The commentator then notes a logical ordering: the argument that kalam involves nazar (reasoning) rather than forbidden jadal refutes the minor premise (that kalam is a bid'ah), whereas the earlier response about bid'ah hasanah refuted the major premise (that all innovation is blameworthy). Thus the order is justified by a general principle of argumentation.

Key Terms
النظر
الجدل
الفكر
المباحثة
الإلزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people equate theological reasoning (nazar) with debate (jadal) as being the same activity.
Correction: The passage clarifies that jadal is specifically for compelling an opponent, while nazar is pure thought. They are distinct in purpose and method.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the primary purpose of jadal (debate)?
A) To seek truth through personal reflection.
B) To compel the opponent (ilzam al-ghayr).
C) To teach others.
D) To memorize theological arguments.

Key Takeaway
Nazar and jadal are distinct: one is reflection, the other is debate aimed at compelling an opponent, and the order of arguments follows logical principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you discuss matters of faith, is your goal to compel others to agree with you (jadal) or to sincerely seek the truth through reflection (nazar)? Which impulse dominates your heart, and what steps can you take today to purify your intention?
?
The lesson shows that arguments have a proper order. In your own understanding of aqeedah, do you build your knowledge step by step, or do you jump into debates without a foundation? What will you change to ensure your convictions are based on solid reasoning?

The Four Opinions on the First Obligatory Duty
Enumeration of four scholarly opinions on the first obligatory duty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four scholarly positions on what the first obligation upon the accountable person is, and the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
The first obligation is debated: knowledge, reflection, or the first step of reflection?

Islamic scholars disagreed on the very first duty that becomes obligatory upon a person who reaches accountability. Is it to know God, to reflect, or something else? This passage from Sanusi's commentary enumerates four distinct opinions with their justifications, showing how foundational this question is for theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the question
Recognize that the passage addresses a fundamental debate: what is the first thing a responsible person (mukallaf) must do?
This sets the stage for the four opinions.
2
Identify the four opinions
Read the Arabic and translation to find each of the four opinions: (1) majority/Ash'ari: knowledge of God, (2) Mu'tazila/Isfarayini: reflection (nazar), (3) some: first part of reflection, (4) al-Qadi/Ibn Furak/Imam al-Haramayn: (incomplete).
Note the reasoning for each.
3
Examine the reasoning
For each opinion, note the cited rationale: knowledge is the root of all religions; reflection is obligatory by consensus and precedes knowledge; obligation of whole entails obligation of parts.
4
Review the commentary's critique
Read the commentary's discussion on the third opinion, where it argues that obligation of the whole does not necessitate obligation of the part.
This is a subtle logical point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السابع : اختلف في أول واجب على المكلف ] ( قد اختلف في أول واجب على المكلف ( أنه ماذا ( فالأكثر) ومنهم الشيخ أبو الحسن الأشعري ) على أنه معرفة الله تعالى إذ هو أصل المعارف ) والعقائد ( الدينية وعليه يتفرع وجوب كل واجب ) من الواجبات الشرعية (وقيل: هو النظر فيها ) أي في معرفة الله سبحانه ( لأنه واجب ( اتفاقاً كما مر ( وهو قبلها ) وهذا مذهب جمهور المعتزلة والأستاذ أبي إسحاق الإسفرائيني ( وقيل : ( هو ( أول جزء من النظر) لأن وجوب الكل يستلزم وجوب أجزائه، فأول جزء من النظر واجب، وهو متقدم على النظر المتقدم على المعرفة ) وقال القاضي : واختاره ابن فورك) وإمام الحرمين أنه
قوله : ( أي في معرفة الله أي لأجل معرفة الله أو في تحصيلها
قوله : ( لأن وجوب الكل إلخ) فيه بحث لأن تعلق الخطاب بالكل أو كونه ممدوحاً مناطاً لاستحقاق الثواب عقلاً، لا يستلزم تعلقه بالجزء أو كونه ممدوحاً مناطاً لاستحقاق الثواب، واللازم التكليف بالكل بدون التكليف بالجزء لا التكليف بالكل بدون الجزء الذي هو محال .
The seventh section: Disagreement on the first obligatory duty upon the accountable person. (Indeed there is disagreement on the first obligatory duty upon the accountable person (what is it? (The majority) and among them Sheikh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari) is that it is knowledge of God Almighty, for it is the origin of religious cognitions and beliefs, and upon it branches the obligation of every obligatory duty from the legal obligations. And it was said: It is reflection (nazar) concerning it, i.e., concerning knowledge of God, because it is obligatory by consensus as has passed, and it is before it. This is the madhhab of the majority of the Mu'tazila and the master Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini. And it was said: It is the first part of reflection, because the obligation of the whole necessitates the obligation of its parts, so the first part of reflection is obligatory, and it precedes reflection which precedes knowledge. And al-Qadi said, and Ibn Furak and Imam al-Haramayn chose it, that... His statement: (i.e., concerning knowledge of God) i.e., for the sake of knowledge of God or for acquiring it. His statement: (because the obligation of the whole etc.) There is discussion in it, because the attachment of the address to the whole, or its being praiseworthy as a basis for deserving reward rationally, does not entail its attachment to the part or its being praiseworthy as a basis for deserving reward. And the necessary consequence is obligation of the whole without obligation of the part, not obligation of the whole without the part, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the four scholarly opinions on the first obligatory duty. The main text lists: (1) The majority, including Ash'ari, say it is knowledge of God, because it is the foundation of all religious beliefs and duties. (2) The Mu'tazila and Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini say it is reflection (nazar) aimed at obtaining knowledge, since reflection is obligatory by consensus and precedes knowledge. (3) Others say it is the first part of reflection, arguing that the obligation of the whole (reflection) entails the obligation of its parts. (4) Al-Qadi, Ibn Furak, and Imam al-Haramayn hold a different opinion, though it is cut off. The commentary then explains that the second opinion's 'nazar' means for the sake of knowledge or acquiring it. For the third opinion, a discussion follows: the obligation of the whole does not rationally entail the obligation of the part, because the address can attach to the whole without attaching to its part; the necessary consequence is obligation of the whole without obligation of the part, which is not impossible.

Key Terms
أول واجب
معرفة الله
نظر
أول جزء من النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the first obligatory duty is the same across all schools.
Correction: In fact, four distinct positions are presented, emphasizing that this is a debated issue.

Checkpoint
Which group held that the first obligatory duty is reflection (nazar)?
A) The Ash'aris
B) The majority
C) The Mu'tazila and Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini
D) The followers of al-Qadi

Key Takeaway
The first obligatory duty is a matter of scholarly disagreement, with four distinct positions centered on knowledge, reflection, and its parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the first obligation is debated, what does your own heart prioritize as the first step in your relationship with God? Is it to know Him, to reflect, or something else? What would change if you took that priority seriously today?
?
The scholars disagreed on whether reflection or knowledge comes first. In your own life, do you tend to act on knowledge without reflecting, or reflect without reaching knowledge? Which imbalance do you need to correct, and what one step can you take this week?
?
The third opinion argues that every whole obligation entails its parts. Are there obligations in your life that you neglect because you focus on the 'whole' without breaking it down? What small first part of a duty can you start with today?

The First Obligation and Its Verbal Resolution
The author states the dispute and explains the verbal resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the verbal nature of the dispute over the first obligation upon the mukallaf and how different definitions lead to different answers.

Core Idea
The dispute over the first obligation is verbal, not substantive.

This lesson addresses a famous point of disagreement among Muslim theologians: what is the very first thing a morally responsible person (mukallaf) must do? The author, Sanusi, explains why the different answers given by various schools actually stem from a verbal confusion rather than a genuine doctrinal difference. By carefully defining what we mean by 'first obligation,' we can see that the dispute dissolves.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Let the author's flow guide you.
Notice that the passage begins with a heading.
2
Distinguish the two senses of 'first obligation'
The author explains that the dispute is verbal because 'first obligation' can mean either the first intended obligation (al-maqsud awwala) or the first absolute obligation. Under each meaning, the answer differs.
Under the first meaning, it is knowledge (ma'rifa) by consensus.
3
Understand the author's handwritten correction
The author notes an addition in his own hand that adds a condition: if we condition possibility, then the first obligation is reflection itself; otherwise, intention.
This formulation is said to encompass all three considered schools.
4
Reflect on the principle of verbal disputes
Consider how many theological disputes are resolved by clarifying the meaning of terms. This is a key principle in kalam.
The passage demonstrates the importance of precise definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد السابع : اختلف في أول واجب على المكلف. القصد إلى النظر لأن النظر فعل اختياري مسبوق بالقصد المتقدم على أول أجزائه. والنزاع لفظي، إذ لو أريد الواجب بالقصد الأول أي لو أريد أول الواجبات المقصودة أولاً وبالذات فهو المعرفة اتفاقاً وإلا أي وإن لم يرد ذلك، بل أريد أول الواجبات مطلقاً فالقصد إلى النظر لأنه مقدمة للنظر الواجب مطلقاً، فيكون واجباً أيضاً وقد عرفت أن وجوب المقدمة إنما يتم في السبب المستلزم دون غيره، ثم أن المصنف الحق في كتابه الذي هو بخطه هكذا وإلا فإن شرطنا كونه مقدورا فالنظر وإلا فالقصد إلى النظر هذا أوفق بسياق الكلام لشموله المذاهب الثلاثة المعتبرة.
The fifth observation - seventh purpose: They differed about the first obligation upon the responsible person. The intention to reflection, because reflection is a voluntary act preceded by intention that comes before its first parts. And the dispute is verbal, for if the obligation is meant by the first intention, that is, if what is meant is the first of the obligations that are intended primarily and essentially, then it is knowledge, by agreement. Otherwise—that is, if that is not meant, but rather the first obligations absolutely—then it is the intention to reflection, because it is a prerequisite for reflection which is obligatory absolutely, so it becomes obligatory as well. And you have known that the obligatoriness of a prerequisite is only established in the case of a necessary cause, not otherwise. Then the author inserted in his book, in his own handwriting, this: 'Otherwise, if we condition it being possible, then it is reflection; otherwise, it is the intention to reflection.' This is more consistent with the context, as it encompasses the three considered schools.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage defines the issue and presents a verbal resolution of the dispute over the first obligation of the mukallaf. It begins by stating that the scholars differed on what is the first obligation. The author then explains that the dispute is verbal: if by 'first obligation' one means the first intended obligation in itself, then it is knowledge (ma'rifa) unanimously. But if 'first obligation' is taken absolutely, the first is the intention (qasd) to reflect, because reflection (nazar) is obligatory, and its prerequisite (intention) is also obligatory. The author then notes a correction by the original text's author, who wrote in his own hand that if we condition possibility, then the first obligation is reflection itself; otherwise, it is intention. This formulation covers the three recognized schools.

Key Terms
أول واجب
النزاع لفظي
القصد الأول
المعرفة
النظر
القصد
المقدمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dispute over the first obligation is a substantive doctrinal difference that cannot be resolved.
Correction: The author clarifies that the dispute is verbal and dissolves once we specify what 'first' means: if intended in itself, it is knowledge; if absolute, it is intention to reflect.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if by 'first obligation' we mean the first intended obligation (al-maqsud awwala wa bil-dhat), what is it?
A) Reflection (nazar)
B) Intention (qasd)
C) Knowledge (ma'rifa)
D) Doubt (shakk)

Key Takeaway
The dispute over the first obligation is resolved by clarifying what is meant by 'first': if intended in itself, it is knowledge; if absolute, it is intention to reflect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a disagreement about Islamic obligations, do you pause to check if the dispute is really about words or about essence? How often have you assumed a substantive conflict when clarifying terms could have resolved it?
?
Reflect on a time you insisted on your view in a religious discussion without first defining your key terms. How might the outcome have changed if you had followed the method of this passage?
?
The passage shows that even the 'first obligation' can be understood differently based on intention. If the very foundational priorities of your faith depend on clear definitions, how seriously do you take precision in your own understanding and speech?

The Mule Example and Razi's Objection
Ibn Sina's example and Razi's objection to the condition of attentiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attentiveness is necessary for inference and the famous infinite regress objection raised by Imam al-Razi.

Core Idea
Attentiveness bridges premises and conclusion; Razi's objection challenges its necessity.

This lesson introduces the concept of tafattun (attentiveness) through a concrete example of a mule that appears pregnant, and the error that arises from lack of attentiveness. It then presents Imam al-Razi's objection that requiring attentiveness as a condition leads to an infinite regress, setting the stage for the answer in the next lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the mule example
Read the passage from 'For one who knows that this is a mule...' up to '...he would be certain that it is barren.' Notice how the person mistakenly thinks the mule is pregnant due to oversight.
The example illustrates that without attentiveness to the subsumption of the particular under the universal, inference fails.
2
Identify Razi's objection
Read al-Razi's argument: the knowledge of subsumption is a separate belief, requiring another premise, leading to infinite regress.
This is a classic objection that if every step of inference requires a separate condition, the process never ends.
3
Reflect on the regress problem
Consider why the infinite regress would prevent attaining knowledge. Think about how each condition would itself need a condition.
The objection challenges the very possibility of inferential knowledge if attentiveness is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكيفية الاندراج) والارتباط بين المقدمتين ( فإن من يعلم أن هذه بغلة وكل بغلة عاقر قد يراها منتفخة البطن، فيظن أنها حامل وما هو أي ظنه كونها حاملا (إلا لذهوله عن ارتباط الصغرى بالكبرى واندراج هذا الجزئي) الذي هو هذه البغلة ( تحت ذلك الكلي ) الذي هو كل بغلة عاقر ، إذ لولا هذا الذهول لجزم بكونها عاقراً، ولم يظن أنها حامل ) ومنعه الإمام الرازي ( فقال : ليس ذلك التفطن شرطاً لإفادة النظر للعلم ( لأن العلم بأن هذا مندرج في ذلك ) وبأن إحدى المقدمتين مرتبطة بالأخرى ( تصديق آخر مغاير للتصديق بالصغرى والكبرى ( فلو وجب العلم به ) أي بأن هذا مندرج في ذاك وبأن هذه مرتبطة بتلك ( كانت ) هذه القضية التي وجب العلم بها ( مقدمة أخرى منضمة إليها ) أي إلى المقدمات الأخر مرتبة معها ( ويجب ملاحظة الترتيب ) وكيفية الاندراج مرة أخرى ويلزم التسلسل فيمتنع حصول العلم بالمطلوب ) والجواب لا نسلم أن ذلك الذي وجب العلم به مقدمة أخرى بل ذلك ) التفطن الذي اعتبره ابن سينا (هو) ملاحظة نسبة المقدمتين إلى النتيجة) فإنه الشرائط المتفق عليها إلا أنه أخره ليكون ذكر الأمور المختلفة في سلك واحد، مع ما فيه من الاهتمام بما قدمه عليه قوله : (التفطن أي التفهم لكيفية الاندراج أي اندراج الأصغر تحت الأوسط إيجاباً أو سلباً كلياً، أو جزئياً مثلاً في قولنا الجسم مركب وكل مركب ممكن بعد التصديق بالمقدمتين لا بد من ملاحظة اندراج الجسم بخصوصه في المركب ليستفاد الحكم عليه بكونه ممكناً، ولولا ذلك بل لوحظ ما صدق عليه الأوسط في الكبرى بعنوان مفهومه إجمالاً، ولم يلاحظ اندراج الأصغر فيه بخصوصه لربما غفل عن النتيجة خصوصاً إذا توهم أمراً مانعاً منها كما نبه عليه الشيخ بالمثال الجزئي المذكور، ثم إن اتصاف ذات الموضوع بمفهومه في القضية تقييد، فيكون ملاحظة الاندراج المذكور تصوراً لا تصديقاً كأنه قيل وكل مركب أي الجسم وغيره المتصف بالتركيب ممكن هذا ملخص كلام الشيخ، وهو حق لا شبهة فيه للمنصف، وبما حررنا لك ظهر اندفاع ما قيل على قوله وهي من قبيل التصور دون التصديق من أن مجرد ملاحظة نسبة المقدمتين إلى النتيجة غير كافية في حصول المطلوب، بل لا بد فيه من الجزم بها والجزم حكم خبري، نعم إن هذا التصديق الحاصل من الهيئة الاجتماعية للمقدمتين، وإن كان تصديقاً آخر مغايراً للمقدمتين لكن لا يلزم وجوب ترتيبه معهما كإيجاب الصغرى وكلية الكبرى. قوله : (هو ملاحظة لنسبة المقدمتين إلخ) أي كيفية اشتمالها عليهما وهي التفطن لكيفية الاندراج. قوله : (المقصد التاسع فيما اختلف في كونه شرطاً للنظر لا يخفى أن حق هذا المقصد أن يلي مباحث الشروط المتفق عليها للمناسبة الظاهرة، فالتخلل بينه وبين تلك المباحث بمباحث أخرى، لا يخلو عن خفاء .
[Concerning the manner of subsumption] and the connection between the two premises: For one who knows that this is a mule and every mule is barren, but sees its belly swollen, so he thinks it is pregnant. And this (his thought that it is pregnant) is only due to his oversight concerning the connection of the minor premise to the major premise and the subsumption of this particular (which is this mule) under that universal (which is every barren mule). For were it not for this oversight, he would be certain that it is barren and would not think it is pregnant. And Imam al-Razi denied this, saying: That attentiveness is not a condition for inference to yield knowledge, because the knowledge that this is subsumed under that and that one premise is connected to the other is another belief (tasdiq) different from the belief in the minor and major premises. So if knowledge of it were necessary, then that proposition would be another premise added to the other premises and must be observed in order, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul), thus preventing the attainment of the desired knowledge. The answer: We do not accept that what is required to be known is another premise; rather, that attentiveness (al-tafattun) which Ibn Sina considered is the observation (mulāhaza) of the relation of the two premises to the conclusion. For it is among the agreed-upon conditions, except that he delayed mentioning it to include various issues in one thread, along with the emphasis on what preceded it. His saying: 'attentiveness (al-tafattun)' means understanding the manner of subsumption, i.e., the subsumption of the minor under the middle term positively or negatively, universally or partially. For example, in our statement 'the body is composite, and every composite is contingent,' after affirming the two premises, one must observe the subsumption of the particular body under 'composite' to derive the judgment that it is contingent. Were it not for that, but rather if one observed what the middle term applies to in the major premise under the aspect of its concept in general and did not observe the subsumption of the particular minor under it individually, one might become oblivious to the conclusion, especially if an obstacle preventing it is imagined, as the teacher (Ibn Sina) pointed out with the particular example. Moreover, the description of the subject's essence by its concept in the proposition is a restriction, so the observation of the mentioned subsumption is a conception (tasawwur), not an assent (tasdiq). As if it were said: 'And every composite' i.e., the body and other things characterized by composition 'is contingent.' This is the summary of the teacher's words, and it is true with no doubt for the impartial. With what we have written for you, what was said about his statement that it is from the type of conception without assent becomes refuted; namely, that mere observation of the relation of the premises to the conclusion is insufficient for attaining the desired knowledge, but rather one must have certainty (jazm) in it, and certainty is a predicative judgment. Yes, this assent that results from the social configuration (al-hay'a al-ijtimā'iyya) of the two premises, even if it is another belief different from the premises, yet it does not necessitate ordering it together with them like the requirement of the minor premise and the universality of the major premise. His saying: 'It is the observation of the relation of the two premises etc.' means the manner in which they comprise them, which is the attentiveness to the manner of subsumption. His saying: 'The ninth maqsad concerning what is differed upon as being a condition for inference' – it is not hidden that the right place for this maqsad is to follow the discussions of the agreed-upon conditions due to the apparent suitability; but its interposition among other discussions is not free from obscurity.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage defines and defends Ibn Sina's condition of tafattun (attentiveness) for inference (nazar) to yield knowledge. It begins with an example: someone who knows that 'this is a mule' and 'every mule is barren' sees a swollen belly and mistakenly thinks the mule is pregnant. The error arises from overlooking the subsumption of this particular mule under the universal 'barren mule'. Imam al-Razi objects that requiring knowledge of this subsumption as a condition leads to an infinite regress, because that knowledge would itself be another premise requiring further subsumption. The answer is that the attentiveness is not a separate premise but merely the observation (mulāhaza) of the relation between the premises and the conclusion. This observation is a conception (tasawwur), not an assent (tasdiq); it does not add another proposition. The author elaborates that such observation is necessary to avoid error, as in the mule example. He explains that the observation of subsumption is a mental act of paying attention, not a new belief. Even though the resulting assent from the configuration of premises is a distinct belief, it does not require ordering as a separate premise. The passage concludes with a note on the placement of this ninth maqsad, suggesting it should follow the agreed-upon conditions.

Key Terms
التفطن
الاندراج
الارتباط
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the mule example, why does the person mistakenly think the mule is pregnant?
A) He does not know the premises.
B) He overlooks the subsumption of the particular mule under the universal.
C) The premises are false.
D) He does not believe the conclusion.

Key Takeaway
Attentiveness to subsumption is necessary for valid inference; al-Razi argues that requiring it leads to an infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you applied a general Islamic principle (e.g., 'Allah knows best' or 'patience is rewarded') to a specific hardship, but still despaired. What prevented you from making the correct logical subsumption in that moment? What concrete step can you take this week to strengthen your attentiveness (tafattun) when facing trials?
?
Razi warns that demanding too many explicit conditions can loop you into infinite regress. Have you ever felt paralyzed in your faith because you kept searching for one more proof before committing to a belief in Allah? How can you trust the clear signs without adding layer upon layer of reasoning? Write down one thing you will stop over-analyzing today and just act on.
?
In the mule example, the person knew the general rule but overlooked its application to the situation. Is there a sin or bad habit you justify because you think your case is exceptional? What general rule from the Quran or Sunnah have you failed to subsume to your own life? Repent now and resolve to apply it literally starting tomorrow.

5 min
The Answer: Attentiveness as Observation
Response to Razi's objection: attentiveness is observation, not a premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars responded to Razi's objection by distinguishing attentiveness as a conception (tasawwur) rather than an additional belief.

Core Idea
Attentiveness is observing the relation, not adding a premise.

This lesson presents the response to al-Razi's infinite regress objection. The key move is that the required attentiveness is not a separate proposition requiring proof; it is merely the mental observation (mulāhaza) of how the premises relate to the conclusion. This observation is a conception (tasawwur), not an assent (tasdiq), so it does not generate a regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the passage starting from 'The answer: We do not accept...' up to '...emphasis on what preceded it.' Pay attention to the distinction between a premise and an observation.
The response denies that the condition adds a new premise.
2
Distinguish conception from assent
Understand that the observation of subsumption is a conception (tasawwur) of the relation, not a separate belief (tasdiq). The author explains that it is like noticing the form of the argument.
A conception does not require its own proof; it is a mental act of attention.
3
See how the regress is avoided
Since the observation is not a proposition, it does not need to be known as a premise, breaking the chain of infinite regress.
Only assents (judgments) would require further conditions; simple noticing does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الجزئي) الذي هو هذه البغلة ( تحت ذلك الكلي ) الذي هو كل بغلة عاقر ، إذ لولا هذا الذهول لجزم بكونها عاقراً، ولم يظن أنها حامل ) ومنعه الإمام الرازي ( فقال : ليس ذلك التفطن شرطاً لإفادة النظر للعلم ( [Concerning the manner of subsumption] and the connection between the two premises: For one who knows that this is a mule and every mule is barren, but sees its belly swollen, so he thinks it is pregnant. And this (his thought that it is pregnant) is only due to his oversight concerning the connection of the minor premise to the major premise and the subsumption of this particular (which is this mule) under that universal (which is every barren mule). For were it not for this oversight, he would be certain that it is barren and would not think it is pregnant. So if knowledge of it were necessary, then that proposition would be another premise added to the other premises and must be observed in order, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul), thus preventing the attainment of the desired knowledge. The answer: We do not accept that what is required to be known is another premise; rather, that attentiveness (al-tafattun) which Ibn Sina considered is the observation (mulāhaza) of the relation of the two premises to the conclusion. For it is among the agreed-upon conditions, except that he delayed mentioning it to include various issues in one thread, along with the emphasis on what preceded it. His saying: 'attentiveness (al-tafattun)' means understanding the manner of subsumption, i.e., the subsumption of the minor under the middle term positively or negatively, universally or partially. For example, in our statement 'the body is composite, and every composite is contingent,' after affirming the two premises, one must observe the subsumption of the particular body under 'composite' to derive the judgment that it is contingent. Were it not for that, but rather if one observed what the middle term applies to in the major premise under the aspect of its concept in general and did not observe the subsumption of the particular minor under it individually, one might become oblivious to the conclusion, especially if an obstacle preventing it is imagined, as the teacher (Ibn Sina) pointed out with the particular example. Moreover, the description of the subject's essence by its concept in the proposition is a restriction, so the observation of the mentioned subsumption is a conception (tasawwur), not an assent (tasdiq). As if it were said: 'And every composite' i.e., the body and other things characterized by composition 'is contingent.' This is the summary of the teacher's words, and it is true with no doubt for the impartial. With what we have written for you, what was said about his statement that it is from the type of conception without assent becomes refuted; namely, that mere observation of the relation of the premises to the conclusion is insufficient for attaining the desired knowledge, but rather one must have certainty (jazm) in it, and certainty is a predicative judgment. Yes, this assent that results from the social configuration (al-hay'a al-ijtimā'iyya) of the two premises, even if it is another belief different from the premises, yet it does not necessitate ordering it together with them like the requirement of the minor premise and the universality of the major premise. His saying: 'It is the observation of the relation of the two premises etc.' means the manner in which they comprise them, which is the attentiveness to the manner of subsumption. His saying: 'The ninth maqsad concerning what is differed upon as being a condition for inference' – it is not hidden that the right place for this maqsad is to follow the discussions of the agreed-upon conditions due to the apparent suitability; but its interposition among other discussions is not free from obscurity.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage defines and defends Ibn Sina's condition of tafattun (attentiveness) for inference (nazar) to yield knowledge. It begins with an example: someone who knows that 'this is a mule' and 'every mule is barren' sees a swollen belly and mistakenly thinks the mule is pregnant. The error arises from overlooking the subsumption of this particular mule under the universal 'barren mule'. Imam al-Razi objects that requiring knowledge of this subsumption as a condition leads to an infinite regress, because that knowledge would itself be another premise requiring further subsumption. The answer is that the attentiveness is not a separate premise but merely the observation (mulāhaza) of the relation between the premises and the conclusion. This observation is a conception (tasawwur), not an assent (tasdiq); it does not add another proposition. The author elaborates that such observation is necessary to avoid error, as in the mule example. He explains that the observation of subsumption is a mental act of paying attention, not a new belief. Even though the resulting assent from the configuration of premises is a distinct belief, it does not require ordering as a separate premise. The passage concludes with a note on the placement of this ninth maqsad, suggesting it should follow the agreed-upon conditions.

Key Terms
ملاحظة
تصور
تصديق
نسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the condition of attentiveness is a separate piece of knowledge requiring independent proof.
Correction: The passage clarifies that attentiveness is not a premise but the observation of the relation, which is a conception, not an additional belief.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what is the nature of the required attentiveness?
A) It is a new premise that must be proven.
B) It is an observation (mulāhaza) of the relation of premises to conclusion.
C) It is a separate belief (tasdiq) about subsumption.
D) It is a doubt about the premises.

Key Takeaway
Attentiveness is a mental observation, not a proposition; therefore it does not generate an infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reason about matters of faith, do you take the time to observe the subsumption of details under general truths, or do you rush to conclusions? What might you be missing by skipping this mental observation?
?
The passage distinguishes conception from assent. In your own intellectual life, do you often confuse understanding an argument with believing it? How can you ensure you are not mistaking one for the other?
?
The infinite regress is stopped by recognizing attentiveness as observation, not a premise. Reflect on a time when you engaged in endless reasoning. Could it be that you were seeking an extra premise when all that was needed was careful observation? How will you change your approach to such questions?

The Core Question and Imam al-Razi's Distinction
Imam al-Razi's initial statement and the framing of the question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the central question of whether knowledge of the indication is distinct from knowledge of the indicated, and Imam al-Razi's argument for their distinctness.

Core Idea
Knowledge of the indication is distinct from knowledge of the indicated.

Imam al-Razi lays out a clear distinction between evidence, indicated, and indication. He argues that because these three are distinct, the knowledges related to them are necessarily distinct as well. This lesson examines that foundational claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the question and al-Razi's view
Read from 'المقصد العاشر' through 'متغايرة أيضاً' in Arabic, then the translation. Focus on the three elements: evidence, indicated, and indication.
Notice how al-Razi uses 'لا شك أنها متغايرة'—no doubt they are distinct.
2
Identify the three elements
List the evidence (mustalzim), the indicated (lazim), and the indication (dalala) as mentioned in the text.
This sets the basis for the whole discussion.
3
Reflect on knowledge distinctness
Ask: If the objects are distinct, must the knowledge of them also be distinct? Al-Razi says yes.
Consider how this connects to the broader question of the lesson.

العالم ووجه دلالته ) هو ( الحدوث ) أو الإمكان ) وهو مغاير له عارض وقال آخرون : لا يجب ذلك ) أي قوله : (قد اختلف إلخ) وجه الاختلاف في مغايرة العلم بالدلالة للعلم بالمدلول غير ظاهر، أن الدليل الذي ذكره الحقيقة، فهو ممنوع لأن الدليل ما يمكن التوصل بصحيح النظر فيه أو في أحواله والنظر لا يقع في وجه دلالته . قوله : ( وقال آخرون : لا يجب ذلك بل قد يدل إلخ فإن قلت : ظاهر هذين الكلامين يدل على جواز المغايرة في بعض الصفات، وآخر الكلام من كونه مبنياً على ما قاله المشايخ يدل على عدم الجواز، قلت لو سلم ادعاء البناء الحقيقي فلا نسلم دلالته على عدم جواز المغايرة أصلاً، إذ المشايخ رحمهم الله لا يدعون الحدوث غير العالم فالاستدلال قد يكون بنفس الحدوث، فحينئذ لا مغايرة بين وجه الدلالة والدليل فلا وجوب وقولهم الحدوث ليس غير العالم إلى استدلال منعي من وجه نعم لو قدم هذا لكان أنسب، فعلى هذا التوجيه ترتبط سوابق الكلام ولواحقه فتأمل. صفة منفكة قوله : (ليس غير العالم مبني على ما أشعر به كلام الفرقة الأولى القائلة بمغايرة الحدوث
The Tenth Purpose: Does knowledge of the indication (al-'ilm bi-dalala) of the evidence (dalil) on the indicated (madlul) differ from knowledge of the indicated? Imam al-Razi said: There is an evidence (mustalzim, e.g., existence of the world) and an indicated (lazim, e.g., existence of the Maker) and an indication (dalala) which is a relation (nisba) between them posterior to them, and there is no doubt that they are distinct; thus the knowledges relating to them are also distinct. Then a group said: The aspect of indication (wajh al-dalala) is other than the evidence, just as we say: The world indicates the existence of the Maker by its temporality (huduth) or its possibility (imkan). So the evidence is the world, and its aspect of indication is temporality or possibility, which is other than it, an accident. And others said: That is not necessary. Meaning: He said: (The disagreement, etc.) — The reason for the disagreement concerning the distinctness of the knowledge of the indication from the knowledge of the indicated is not apparent, because the evidence mentioned by the Imam implies its distinctness from the knowledge of the evidence and the knowledge of the indicated, in a way not hidden from anyone with minimal discernment, and similarly there is no confusion about the distinctness of the aspect of indication, i.e., the matter by means of which the mind transfers from the evidence to the indicated of the evidence, for its definition proclaims its distinctness, so how did it escape the great scholars and how did they disagree about it? He said: (It is not necessary, etc.) — This, and his saying 'rather it may indicate, etc.' is explicit that these people claimed the removal of necessity. He said: (For it also is an evidence, etc.) — There is discussion in it: because if it is based on that possibility (imkan) is part of the world, then it would be evidence for the existence of the Maker; to which it is objected that we do not accept that because it is a conceptual matter (amr i'tibari). And (if based on) that what is evidence for the existence of the Maker must have its aspect of indication, on the supposition of distinctness, be an evidence; infinite regress (tasalsul) would only follow if the aspect of indication of every evidence were an evidence; it is permissible to end at an evidence whose aspect of indication is not evidence for anything. And if it is based on the fact that since the evidence is evidence by virtue of that aspect, the aspect is in reality an evidence, then it is denied because the evidence is that which can be reached by sound consideration (nazar) in it or in its states, and consideration does not fall upon its aspect of indication. He said: (And others said: That is not necessary, rather it may indicate, etc.) — Then you might say: The apparent meaning of these two statements indicates the permissibility of distinctness in some attributes, and the later part of the statement, being based on what the Mashayikh said, indicates the impermissibility of distinctness. I say: Even if we accept the claim of real construction (bina' haqiqi), we do not accept its implication of the impermissibility of distinctness absolutely. For the Mashayikh (may Allah have mercy on them) do not claim of every attribute of a thing that it is neither it nor other; rather the attribute according to them may differ from the qualified if it is separable (munfakka) from its qualified, and may not differ if it is inseparable (mula'ima) and does not separate from it, as the commentator will quote from al-Amidi in the sixth purpose of the fourth watchtower regarding unity, multiplicity, and relation in his saying: 'The attribute of a thing is neither it nor other' — this is for the covenantal (al-'ahd) and what is meant is the essential attribute (al-sifa al-lazima). So mere construction on what the Mashayikh mentioned and the saying that the aspect of indication is an attribute of the evidence does not necessitate non-distinctness; for the aspect of indication may be an attribute of the evidence separable from it, like temporality (al-huduth) meaning emergence from non-existence into existence, on the supposition of its existence, for it is from the originated being (al-hadith), as you will see in the second purpose of the fourth watchtower on existential attributes, and may not be separable from it like possibility. And know that the seventh group (al-firqa al-sabi'a) claimed that the aspect of indication — e.g., temporality — is other than the evidence, which is the world absolutely. So the statement of the second group, who deny necessity — 'rather the thing may indicate' — is a reference to a concessive argument (istidlal taslimi) for the denial of necessity, i.e., if we accept that temporality is other than the world, then the inference might be by temporality itself; then there would be no distinctness between the aspect of indication and the evidence, so no necessity. And their statement 'temporality is not other than the world' is a prevenient argument (istidlal man'i) from a certain perspective. Yes, if this had been presented first, it would have been more appropriate. And upon this interpretation, the antecedents and consequents of the speech are connected, so reflect. A separable attribute. He said: (Not other than the world is based on what the statement of the first group, who affirm the distinctness of temporality, gives to understand.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the tenth purpose of the fifth watchtower, which asks whether knowledge of the indication of the evidence for the indicated differs from knowledge of the indicated itself. The commentator presents the question and three positions: (1) Imam al-Razi's view that the evidence, the indicated, and the indication are all distinct, leading to distinct knowledges; (2) a group holding that the aspect of indication (wajh al-dalala) is different from the evidence; and (3) another group denying that necessity. The commentator then explains that the reason for disagreement is obscure, since Imam al-Razi's argument seems evident. He discusses the objection regarding infinite regress (tasalsul) that would follow if every aspect of indication were itself an evidence, and he offers a detailed rebuttal, explaining that it is possible for an aspect not to be evidence. He also addresses the claim that the aspect of indication is an attribute of the evidence, showing that attributes may be separable (like temporality) or inseparable (like possibility), and that this does not determine distinctness. Finally, he clarifies the position of the seventh group and the concessive argument of the second group.

العلم بالمدلول

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Imam al-Razi insists that knowledge of the indication is distinct from knowledge of the indicated. In your own spiritual journey, have you ever confused learning about the proofs for God with actually knowing God? How can you ensure your study of evidence leads you to a deeper personal connection with the Indicated?

What Is Kalam?

The Aspect of Indication: Disagreement on Distinctness
Presentation of the two views on wajh al-dalala.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the 'aspect of indication' (wajh al-dalala) is and why there is disagreement about whether it is distinct from the evidence.

Core Idea
The aspect of indication may or may not be distinct from the evidence itself.

After al-Razi's initial claim, two further views emerge: one group holds that the aspect of indication (like temporality) is different from the evidence, while another group denies that this is necessary. This lesson explores this disagreement and the reason behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the discussion of the two groups
Read from 'ثم قال قوم' through 'لا يجب ذلك' in Arabic. Focus on the two positions: one affirming distinctness, the other denying necessity.
Notice the phrase 'وجه الدلالة غير الدليل' – the aspect is other than the evidence.
2
Identify the aspect of indication
Find examples given: temporality (huduth) and possibility (imkan) as aspects of the world's evidence.
These are attributes or qualities of the evidence that serve as the connection.
3
Reflect on the disagreement's obscurity
The commentator says the reason for disagreement is not apparent ('غير ظاهر'). Think about why such a seemingly clear issue could be disputed.
Consider that the aspect of indication might be considered part of the evidence in some accounts.

ترتبط سوابق الكلام ولواحقه فتأمل. صفة منفكة قوله : (ليس غير العالم مبني على ما أشعر به كلام الفرقة الأولى القائلة بمغايرة الحدوث
The Tenth Purpose: Does knowledge of the indication (al-'ilm bi-dalala) of the evidence (dalil) on the indicated (madlul) differ from knowledge of the indicated? Imam al-Razi said: There is an evidence (mustalzim, e.g., existence of the world) and an indicated (lazim, e.g., existence of the Maker) and an indication (dalala) which is a relation (nisba) between them posterior to them, and there is no doubt that they are distinct; thus the knowledges relating to them are also distinct. Then a group said: The aspect of indication (wajh al-dalala) is other than the evidence, just as we say: The world indicates the existence of the Maker by its temporality (huduth) or its possibility (imkan). So the evidence is the world, and its aspect of indication is temporality or possibility, which is other than it, an accident. And others said: That is not necessary. Meaning: He said: (The disagreement, etc.) — The reason for the disagreement concerning the distinctness of the knowledge of the indication from the knowledge of the indicated is not apparent, because the evidence mentioned by the Imam implies its distinctness from the knowledge of the evidence and the knowledge of the indicated, in a way not hidden from anyone with minimal discernment, and similarly there is no confusion about the distinctness of the aspect of indication, i.e., the matter by means of which the mind transfers from the evidence to the indicated of the evidence, for its definition proclaims its distinctness, so how did it escape the great scholars and how did they disagree about it? He said: (It is not necessary, etc.) — This, and his saying 'rather it may indicate, etc.' is explicit that these people claimed the removal of necessity. He said: (For it also is an evidence, etc.) — There is discussion in it: because if it is based on that possibility (imkan) is part of the world, then it would be evidence for the existence of the Maker; to which it is objected that we do not accept that because it is a conceptual matter (amr i'tibari). And (if based on) that what is evidence for the existence of the Maker must have its aspect of indication, on the supposition of distinctness, be an evidence; infinite regress (tasalsul) would only follow if the aspect of indication of every evidence were an evidence; it is permissible to end at an evidence whose aspect of indication is not evidence for anything. And if it is based on the fact that since the evidence is evidence by virtue of that aspect, the aspect is in reality an evidence, then it is denied because the evidence is that which can be reached by sound consideration (nazar) in it or in its states, and consideration does not fall upon its aspect of indication. He said: (And others said: That is not necessary, rather it may indicate, etc.) — Then you might say: The apparent meaning of these two statements indicates the permissibility of distinctness in some attributes, and the later part of the statement, being based on what the Mashayikh said, indicates the impermissibility of distinctness. I say: Even if we accept the claim of real construction (bina' haqiqi), we do not accept its implication of the impermissibility of distinctness absolutely. For the Mashayikh (may Allah have mercy on them) do not claim of every attribute of a thing that it is neither it nor other; rather the attribute according to them may differ from the qualified if it is separable (munfakka) from its qualified, and may not differ if it is inseparable (mula'ima) and does not separate from it, as the commentator will quote from al-Amidi in the sixth purpose of the fourth watchtower regarding unity, multiplicity, and relation in his saying: 'The attribute of a thing is neither it nor other' — this is for the covenantal (al-'ahd) and what is meant is the essential attribute (al-sifa al-lazima). So mere construction on what the Mashayikh mentioned and the saying that the aspect of indication is an attribute of the evidence does not necessitate non-distinctness; for the aspect of indication may be an attribute of the evidence separable from it, like temporality (al-huduth) meaning emergence from non-existence into existence, on the supposition of its existence, for it is from the originated being (al-hadith), as you will see in the second purpose of the fourth watchtower on existential attributes, and may not be separable from it like possibility. And know that the seventh group (al-firqa al-sabi'a) claimed that the aspect of indication — e.g., temporality — is other than the evidence, which is the world absolutely. So the statement of the second group, who deny necessity — 'rather the thing may indicate' — is a reference to a concessive argument (istidlal taslimi) for the denial of necessity, i.e., if we accept that temporality is other than the world, then the inference might be by temporality itself; then there would be no distinctness between the aspect of indication and the evidence, so no necessity. And their statement 'temporality is not other than the world' is a prevenient argument (istidlal man'i) from a certain perspective. Yes, if this had been presented first, it would have been more appropriate. And upon this interpretation, the antecedents and consequents of the speech are connected, so reflect. A separable attribute. He said: (Not other than the world is based on what the statement of the first group, who affirm the distinctness of temporality, gives to understand.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the tenth purpose of the fifth watchtower, which asks whether knowledge of the indication of the evidence for the indicated differs from knowledge of the indicated itself. The commentator presents the question and three positions: (1) Imam al-Razi's view that the evidence, the indicated, and the indication are all distinct, leading to distinct knowledges; (2) a group holding that the aspect of indication (wajh al-dalala) is different from the evidence; and (3) another group denying that necessity. The commentator then explains that the reason for disagreement is obscure, since Imam al-Razi's argument seems evident. He discusses the objection regarding infinite regress (tasalsul) that would follow if every aspect of indication were itself an evidence, and he offers a detailed rebuttal, explaining that it is possible for an aspect not to be evidence. He also addresses the claim that the aspect of indication is an attribute of the evidence, showing that attributes may be separable (like temporality) or inseparable (like possibility), and that this does not determine distinctness. Finally, he clarifies the position of the seventh group and the concessive argument of the second group.

Key Terms
وجه الدلالة
الحدوث
الإمكان
الفرقة الأولى
الفرقة الثانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the aspect of indication must always be distinct from the evidence.
Correction: The second group denies this necessity, and the commentator shows that it can be otherwise.

Checkpoint
What is the example given for the aspect of indication in the first group's view?
A) The existence of the Maker.
B) The world's temporality or possibility.
C) The relation between evidence and indicated.
D) The knowledge of the evidence.

Key Takeaway
There is disagreement on whether the aspect of indication is always distinct from the evidence; some say it is, others say it is not necessary.

What Is Kalam?

The Infinite Regress Objection and its Rebuttal
Rebuttal of the infinite regress objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection of infinite regress (tasalsul) raised against the view that wajh al-dalala is distinct, and how the commentator rebuts it.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is avoided because the aspect of indication need not be evidence itself.

One objection to the distinctness of wajh al-dalala is that it leads to an infinite regress: if the aspect of indication is itself an evidence, then it would require a further aspect, and so on. The commentator explains why this regress does not necessarily follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and rebuttal
Read from 'قوله : فإنه أيضاً دليل إلخ' through 'لا يقع في وجه دلالته' in Arabic. Focus on the two possible bases for the objection and the rebuttal.
The objection is attributed to those who say 'لا يجب'.
2
Understand the first basis and response
If the objection is based on imkan being part of the world, the commentator says imkan is conceptual (amr i'tibari) and not part of the world, so no regress.
The rebuttal hinges on the nature of possibility.
3
Understand the second basis and response
If the objection is that the aspect is evidence because it is that by which the evidence is evidence, the commentator says nazar (consideration) does not fall on the aspect itself, so it is not an evidence.
The key is that the aspect is not directly considered in inference.
4
Grasp the permissible endpoint
The commentator states that it is permissible to stop at an evidence whose aspect of indication is not itself evidence for anything.
This stops the regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

العالم ووجه دلالته ) هو ( الحدوث ) أو الإمكان ) وهو مغاير له عارض وقال آخرون : لا يجب ذلك ) أي قوله : (قد اختلف إلخ) وجه الاختلاف في مغايرة العلم بالدلالة للعلم بالمدلول غير ظاهر، أن الدليل الذي ذكره الحقيقة، فهو ممنوع لأن الدليل ما يمكن التوصل بصحيح النظر فيه أو في أحواله والنظر لا يقع في وجه دلالته . قوله : ( وقال آخرون : لا يجب ذلك بل قد يدل إلخ فإن قلت : ظاهر هذين الكلامين يدل على جواز Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the condition for infinite regress to follow?
A) The aspect of indication is distinct from the evidence.
B) The aspect of indication of every evidence is itself an evidence.
C) The world is not evidence for the Maker.
D) Possibility is a real attribute.

Key Takeaway
The objection of infinite regress is answered by showing that the aspect of indication need not be evidence itself, and it is permissible to terminate the chain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself demanding an endless chain of proofs for your faith, never satisfied with any single evidence? This lesson shows that the chain can stop. Will you now accept that the aspect of indication need not be proven itself, and let your nazar rest on the evidence alone?
?
Do you treat mental concepts like 'possibility' as if they were real things, causing confusion in your reasoning? This lesson calls imkan a 'conceptual matter.' Can you identify an area where you have confused a mental construct with reality, and commit to correcting that distinction?
?
The scholar argues that nazar does not fall on the aspect of indication. Is there an aspect of your belief that you have been trying to analyze and prove unnecessarily, when you could simply accept it as part of the evidence? Will you stop that intellectual striving and trust the evidence as it is?

Attributes: Separable and Inseparable Resolving the Disagreement
Explanation of separable and inseparable attributes and their role in the disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the distinction between separable (munfakka) and inseparable (lazima) attributes helps resolve the disagreement about wajh al-dalala.

Core Idea
Whether the aspect of indication is distinct from the evidence depends on whether it is a separable or inseparable attribute.

The commentator introduces the concept of separable vs. inseparable attributes to clarify the disagreement. Temporality (huduth) is separable, while possibility (imkan) is inseparable. This explains why the necessity of distinctness is not absolute.

Guided Path
1
Read the discussion of attributes
Read from 'قوله : وقال آخرون : لا يجب ذلك بل قد يدل إلخ' through 'وقد لا تكون منفكة عنه كالإمكان' in Arabic. Focus on the distinction between separable and inseparable attributes.
The commentator references the Mashayikh and al-Amidi.
2
Identify the two types of attributes
Find the definitions: separable (munfakka) can be separated from the object; inseparable (lazima) cannot be separated.
Temporality is given as an example of separable, possibility as inseparable.
3
Apply to wajh al-dalala
If wajh is a separable attribute (like huduth), it is distinct from the evidence; if inseparable (like imkan), it is not distinct.
Thus, the disagreement is based on which type of attribute the aspect is.
4
Understand the seventh group's position
Read the final part about the seventh group (الفرقة السابعة) who claim wajh is absolutely other than the evidence.
The commentator explains their reasoning and how the second group responds with a concessive argument.

الحقيقة، فهو ممنوع لأن الدليل ما يمكن التوصل بصحيح النظر فيه أو في أحواله والنظر لا يقع في وجه دلالته . قوله : ( وقال آخرون : لا يجب ذلك بل قد يدل إلخ فإن قلت : ظاهر هذين الكلامين يدل على جواز المغايرة في بعض الصفات، وآخر الكلام من كونه مبنياً على ما قاله المشايخ يدل على عدم الجواز، قلت لو سلم ادعاء البناء الحقيقي فلا نسلم دلالته على عدم جواز المغايرة أصلاً، إذ المشايخ رحمهم الله لا يدعون في كل صفة للشيء أنها لا هو ولا غيره، بل الصفة عندهم قد تغاير الموصوف إذا كانت منفكة عن موصوفها وقد لا تغاير بأن تلازمه ولا تنفك عنه كما سينقله الشارح عن الآمدي في المقصد السادس من المرصد الرابع في الوحدة، والكثرة والإضافة في قوله صفة الشيء لا هو ولا غيره للعهد والمراد الصفة اللازمة فمجرد البناء على ما ذكره المشايخ والقول بأن وجه الدلالة صفة للدليل لا يلزمه عدم المغايرة إذ قد يكون وجه الدلالة صفة للدليل منفكة عنه، كالحدوث بمعنى الخروج من العدم إلى الوجود على تقدير وجوده فإنه عن الحادث، كما ستقف عليه في المقصد الثاني من المرصد الرابع في الصفات الوجودية، وقد لا تكون منفكة عنه كالإمكان، واعلم أن الفرقة السابعة ادعوا أن وجه الدلالة وهي الحدوث مثلاً غير الدليل، وهو العالم البتة فقول الفرقة الثانية القائلين بنفي الوجوب، بل قد يدلّ الشيء إشارة إلى استدلال تسليمي على نفي الوجوب إلخ أي لو سلم أن الحدوث غير العالم فالاستدلال قد يكون بنفس الحدوث، فحينئذ لا مغايرة بين وجه الدلالة والدليل فلا وجوب وقولهم الحدوث ليس غير العالم إلى استدلال منعي من وجه نعم لو قدم هذا لكان أنسب، فعلى هذا التوجيه ترتبط سوابق الكلام ولواحقه فتأمل. صفة منفكة قوله : (ليس غير العالم مبني على ما أشعر به كلام الفرقة الأولى القائلة بمغايرة الحدوث
The Tenth Purpose: Does knowledge of the indication (al-'ilm bi-dalala) of the evidence (dalil) on the indicated (madlul) differ from knowledge of the indicated? Imam al-Razi said: There is an evidence (mustalzim, e.g., existence of the world) and an indicated (lazim, e.g., existence of the Maker) and an indication (dalala) which is a relation (nisba) between them posterior to them, and there is no doubt that they are distinct; thus the knowledges relating to them are also distinct. Then a group said: The aspect of indication (wajh al-dalala) is other than the evidence, just as we say: The world indicates the existence of the Maker by its temporality (huduth) or its possibility (imkan). So the evidence is the world, and its aspect of indication is temporality or possibility, which is other than it, an accident. And others said: That is not necessary. Meaning: He said: (The disagreement, etc.) — The reason for the disagreement concerning the distinctness of the knowledge of the indication from the knowledge of the indicated is not apparent, because the evidence mentioned by the Imam implies its distinctness from the knowledge of the evidence and the knowledge of the indicated, in a way not hidden from anyone with minimal discernment, and similarly there is no confusion about the distinctness of the aspect of indication, i.e., the matter by means of which the mind transfers from the evidence to the indicated of the evidence, for its definition proclaims its distinctness, so how did it escape the great scholars and how did they disagree about it? He said: (It is not necessary, etc.) — This, and his saying 'rather it may indicate, etc.' is explicit that these people claimed the removal of necessity. He said: (For it also is an evidence, etc.) — There is discussion in it: because if it is based on that possibility (imkan) is part of the world, then it would be evidence for the existence of the Maker; to which it is objected that we do not accept that because it is a conceptual matter (amr i'tibari). And (if based on) that what is evidence for the existence of the Maker must have its aspect of indication, on the supposition of distinctness, be an evidence; infinite regress (tasalsul) would only follow if the aspect of indication of every evidence were an evidence; it is permissible to end at an evidence whose aspect of indication is not evidence for anything. And if it is based on the fact that since the evidence is evidence by virtue of that aspect, the aspect is in reality an evidence, then it is denied because the evidence is that which can be reached by sound consideration (nazar) in it or in its states, and consideration does not fall upon its aspect of indication. He said: (And others said: That is not necessary, rather it may indicate, etc.) — Then you might say: The apparent meaning of these two statements indicates the permissibility of distinctness in some attributes, and the later part of the statement, being based on what the Mashayikh said, indicates the impermissibility of distinctness. I say: Even if we accept the claim of real construction (bina' haqiqi), we do not accept its implication of the impermissibility of distinctness absolutely. For the Mashayikh (may Allah have mercy on them) do not claim of every attribute of a thing that it is neither it nor other; rather the attribute according to them may differ from the qualified if it is separable (munfakka) from its qualified, and may not differ if it is inseparable (mula'ima) and does not separate from it, as the commentator will quote from al-Amidi in the sixth purpose of the fourth watchtower regarding unity, multiplicity, and relation in his saying: 'The attribute of a thing is neither it nor other' — this is for the covenantal (al-'ahd) and what is meant is the essential attribute (al-sifa al-lazima). So mere construction on what the Mashayikh mentioned and the saying that the aspect of indication is an attribute of the evidence does not necessitate non-distinctness; for the aspect of indication may be an attribute of the evidence separable from it, like temporality (al-huduth) meaning emergence from non-existence into existence, on the supposition of its existence, for it is from the originated being (al-hadith), as you will see in the second purpose of the fourth watchtower on existential attributes, and may not be separable from it like possibility. And know that the seventh group (al-firqa al-sabi'a) claimed that the aspect of indication — e.g., temporality — is other than the evidence, which is the world absolutely. So the statement of the second group, who deny necessity — 'rather the thing may indicate' — is a reference to a concessive argument (istidlal taslimi) for the denial of necessity, i.e., if we accept that temporality is other than the world, then the inference might be by temporality itself; then there would be no distinctness between the aspect of indication and the evidence, so no necessity. And their statement 'temporality is not other than the world' is a prevenient argument (istidlal man'i) from a certain perspective. Yes, if this had been presented first, it would have been more appropriate. And upon this interpretation, the antecedents and consequents of the speech are connected, so reflect. A separable attribute. He said: (Not other than the world is based on what the statement of the first group, who affirm the distinctness of temporality, gives to understand.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the tenth purpose of the fifth watchtower, which asks whether knowledge of the indication of the evidence for the indicated differs from knowledge of the indicated itself. The commentator presents the question and three positions: (1) Imam al-Razi's view that the evidence, the indicated, and the indication are all distinct, leading to distinct knowledges; (2) a group holding that the aspect of indication (wajh al-dalala) is different from the evidence; and (3) another group denying that necessity. The commentator then explains that the reason for disagreement is obscure, since Imam al-Razi's argument seems evident. He discusses the objection regarding infinite regress (tasalsul) that would follow if every aspect of indication were itself an evidence, and he offers a detailed rebuttal, explaining that it is possible for an aspect not to be evidence. He also addresses the claim that the aspect of indication is an attribute of the evidence, showing that attributes may be separable (like temporality) or inseparable (like possibility), and that this does not determine distinctness. Finally, he clarifies the position of the seventh group and the concessive argument of the second group.

Key Terms
صفة منفكة

الفرقة السابعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all attributes are either identical to the object or distinct in the same way.
Correction: Attributes are of two types: separable (distinct) and inseparable (not distinct), and the aspect of indication can be either.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is given as an example of a separable attribute?
A) Possibility (imkan).
B) Temporality (huduth).
C) The evidence itself.
D) The indicated.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between separable and inseparable attributes resolves the disagreement: wajh al-dalala may be distinct if it is a separable attribute (like temporality) or not distinct if inseparable (like possibility).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own reasoning about Allah's existence, do you treat the attribute of 'temporality of the world' as separable from the world itself? If it were inseparable, would your trust in the proof be shaken? Honestly, what would that reveal about the foundation of your certainty? This week, before you use any proof, ask yourself: 'Is the aspect I am pointing to inseparable from its evidence?' If not, how can you be sure it points where you claim? Do not ignore this subtle distinction—it may be the crack through which doubt enters.
?
The second group offered both a concessive and a preventive argument. Which style do you naturally prefer when defending your beliefs—granting a point to show it leads to your conclusion, or outright denying the premise? Examine your temperament: is it pride or humility that drives your choice? Before your next theological discussion, decide intentionally which approach you will take, and pay attention to whether you can genuinely concede without losing your ground.
?
The seventh group asserted absolutely that temporality is other than the world. Do you hold any of your own theological beliefs with such absoluteness? Are you willing to assert that the aspect you rely on in a proof is fully distinct, even if it exposes you to counterarguments? If not, what is the cost of your hesitation? Today, pick one belief you hold about Allah's attributes and ask yourself: 'Is this attribute separable from His essence as I conceive it?' Let the answer unsettle you until you find rest either in distinction or in union—but do not remain comfortable in ambiguity.
